2025-05-20 14:42:14 - WARNING: ['Polyurethanes synthesized from diisocyanate trimers and polyethylene glycol undergo crosslinking via thermally reversible Diels–Alder reactions', 'Heru Santoso1,2 · Sabrina Aufar Salma2 · Frita Yuliati2 · Shayrilla Chairunnisa Nur Afifa3 · Sumarno Sumarno1', 'Received: 11 November 2024 / Revised: 17 February 2025 / Accepted: 7 March 2025  © The Author(s), under exclusive licence to The Polymer Society of Korea 2025', 'Abstract', 'A series of self-healing polyurethane (PU) polymers were synthesized via the Diels–Alder reaction between the furan-functionalized PU prepolymer and bismaleimide (BMI). The PU prepolymer is a reaction product of polyethylene glycol (PEG) and a pentamethylene diisocyanate trimer (PDI-t). Furfuryl amine was attached to the terminal isocyanic groups of PU, resulting in furfuryl amine polyurethane (PU-FA). PU-FA was cross-linked via dynamic bonds between the furan groups and BMI, generating reversible bonds with the material. This study explored reversible polymer networks under various thermal conditions, including different reaction parameters, prepolymer ratios, and healing temperatures, to evaluate the healing capabilities of these polymers. The results demonstrated that PU-DA exhibits thermal reversibility, as evidenced by the appearance of an endothermic peak in the DSC analysis, indicating bond cleavage via the retro-Diels–Alder (r-DA) reaction. This endothermic peak re-emerged after cooling at 70 °C for 24 h, further confirming the reversibility of the reac-tion. Additionally, crack healing in the PU-DA film was facilitated by the synergistic effects of the thermally reversible Diels–Alder reaction and the thermal mobility of molecular chains. The self-healing performance evaluation revealed that PU-DA samples with an NCO/OH ratio of 1.5 exhibited a reduction in tensile strength after healing compared to the original PU-DA specimens before heat treatment. Conversely, samples with an NCO/OH ratio of 3 demonstrated a healing efficiency exceeding 100%, attributed to the enhanced cross-link density, which contributed to a more robust network structure.', '@', '@', "In this study, self-healing Diels-Alder polyurethane (PU-DA) was successfully synthesized using PDI trimer. The findings confirmed that the material's healing mechanism is activated by the reversible interaction between furan and maleimide groups within PU-DA, enabling it to autonomously repair structural damage", 'Keywords Polyurethane · Prepolymer · Self-healing · Pentamethylene diisocyanate trimer · Diels–Alder', '1 Introduction', 'Polyurethane has profoundly improved the quality of modern life through its diverse applications across multiple industries. It is integral in enhancing the performance and functionality of products in sectors, such as coatings, automotive, textiles, synthetic leather, electronics, construction materials, aerospace, and medicine. This versatile material not only improves the durability and efficiency of these products but also contributes to advances in technology and quality of life [1–4]. In practical applications, polyurethane is susceptible to the development of small cracks due to factors, such as heat, mechanical stress, and exposure to chemicals. These microcracks can significantly reduce the service life of polyurethane and are often challenging to detect visually [5]. Inspired by the self-repair mechanisms observed in nature, scientists have engineered polymers with inherent self-healing capabilities within certain limits. Self-healing strategies can be broadly categorized as extrinsic or intrinsic [6]. Extrinsic self-healing involves the incorporation of a healing agent within a polymer matrix; however, this method lacks reusability, because the healing agent is depleted upon each use. With this method, no external stimuli are needed for healing to occur. Conversely, the intrinsic self-healing system enables repetitive healing across multiple cycles of damage, although most methods in this system require external stimuli to initiate the healing process. As a result, intrinsic self-healing materials have attracted increasing attention in recent years [7].', 'Intrinsic self-healing materials normally contain dynamic bonds or interactions that can be broken and reformed to heal the material. These bonds can be covalent or nonco-valent in nature. Covalent bonds are often used because of their high strength of the bonds. Some examples of these bonds are Diels–Alder, disulfide, radical interactions, and cyclic compounds [8, 9]. Numerous studies have been conducted to develop self-healing polyurethanes with Diels–Alder chemistry. Self-healing polyurethanes (SH-PU) via the Diels–Alder method mostly comprise isocyanic polyurethane (to differ from nonisocyanic polyurethane) and commonly employ two main approaches. The first method involves the incorporation of furan terminals into the PU prepolymer, which are subsequently crosslinked with bis-maleimide (BMI). The second method involves the prepa-ration of a PU prepolymer containing maleimide pendant groups, which consequently react with difunctional furan compounds to produce SH-PU [5].', 'For isocyanates, researchers prefer the use of aliphatic isocyanate trimers for polyurethane coatings, because they offer lower toxicity, enhanced thermal stability, superior mechanical properties, and increased weather resistance [10]. The use of isocyanate trimers in the synthesis of self-healing polyurethane via the Diels–Alder system is wide-spread among researchers, with isophorone diisocyanate (IPDI) trimers being the most commonly used [11–13]. IPDI is a cycloaliphatic isocyanate with one ring structure, whereas the IPDI trimer comprises a structure with four rings. These rings make the obtained polyurethane more rigid than when the diisocyanate is fully aliphatic, while more flexible materials are needed. To fill this gap, the entirely aliphatic hexamethylene diisocyanate (HDI) and its trimers (HDI-t) were used. Researchers have used HDI-t to fabricate self-healing polymers [14–22]. To answer the demand for greener materials, the bio-based pentamethyl-ene diisocyanate and its trimer are already available in the market. Some researchers have employed pentamethylene diisocyanate trimers (PDI-t) in the synthesis of functional polyurethanes [23–25]. Most studies on self-healing polyu-rethanes primarily use the conventional isocyanates derived from fossil resources, such as 1,6-diisocyanatohexane (HDI), which does not align with the growing demand for more environmentally sustainable and eco-friendly materials [26, 27]. Despite its promising potential to improve cross-linking density and mechanical properties, the use of PDI-trimer derived from bio-based sources as an isocyanate has not been widely explored. In this study, we investigated the self-healing synthesis of PDI-trimer as an isocyanate through the Diels–Alder reaction method, focusing on the impact of the NCO/OH ratio on its thermal behavior, healing efficiency, and mechanical performance. A series of PU prepolymer formulations were synthesized from polyethylene glycol (PEG) and PDI-trimer (PDI-t) at different reaction times, temperatures, and NCO/OH ratios. These prepolymers were then reacted with furfuryl amine (FA) to yield PU-FA. Addi-tionally, self-healing polyurethane (SH-PU) was synthesized by cross-linking PU-FA with bismaleimide (BMI) via the Diels–Alder reaction. These experiments were performed to understand how the different synthesis conditions affect the mechanical properties and healing behavior of the end polymer products. The knowledge is expected to contribute to the development of green polyurethane, particularly for coating application.', '2 \x07Experimental', '2.1 \x07Materials', "Furfurylamine (99%, FA), 1,1'-(methylenedi-4,1-phenylene) bismaleimide (BMI), polyethylene glycol 4000 (PEG), and dibutyltin dilaurate (95%, DBTDL) were obtained from Sigma-Aldrich. Before polymerization, polyethylene glycol 4000 (PEG) with a molecular weight of 4000 g/mol was dried in a vacuum oven at 120 °C for 2 h. The isocyanate trimer Desmodur® eco N7300 (pentamethylene diisocy-anate/PDI-t), with an NCO content of 21.9% and an equiva-lent weight of 195, was sourced directly from PT Covestro Indonesia without further purification. Dimethylformamide (DMF), which was used as the solvent in the procedure, was purchased from Merck. Polyurethane with a furan terminal (PU-FA) and self-healing polyurethane (PU-DA) were syn-thesized as described in the previous studies [11, 28]. The synthesis details are described in the supporting information. FTIR spectroscopy spectra were acquired using a Brucker Tensor 27 spectrometer via the attenuated total reflectance (ATR) method, which uses 64 scans with a resolution of 4 \xadcm − 1 in transmittance mode in the wavenumber range of 4000–500 \xadcm − 1. Proton nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) analysis was conducted using a Bruker Avance Neo 700 MHz NMR spectrophotometer with DMSO-d6 and \xadCDCl3 as solvents. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) of PU-furan and PU-DA was performed using a Linseis STA instrument (STA PT1600) under a nitrogen atmosphere at a heating rate of 20 °C min1 from 30 to 900 °C. The thermal behavior of PU-DA was assessed via differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) using a Perkin Elmer DSC 8000 instru-ment in the temperature range of 0–190 °C. The heating and cooling rates were 20 °C/min, and all experiments were conducted under a protective nitrogen atmosphere. The melting temperature \xad(Tm) and entropy of fusion (ΔHm) data were collected during the second heating run. The density of the crosslinked polyurethanes (PUs) was determined using a pycnometer with distilled water as the immersion liquid. To assess the crosslink density of the samples, the degree of swelling, and gel content, the samples were measured. Samples 10 × 10x0.3 mm in size were immersed in 10 mL of toluene at room temperature in sealed containers for 24 h. Afterward, the film was washed with toluene and weighed to determine its mass \xad(ms). The films were subsequently sub-jected to drying at 35 °C initially under atmospheric pressure and then under vacuum, after which the weight of the dried film \xad(md) was recorded. The degree of swelling (Q) and gel content (G) were calculated using Eqs. (1) and (2) [29, 30]", '2.2 \x07Characterization', 'Q = 1 + 휌1휌2( msmd −1).', 'Furthermore', 'G(%) = mdmiso × 100.', 'Here, ρ1 and ρ2 represent the densities of the solvent (tol-uene, ρ: 0.8669 g/cm3) and the PU polymer, respectively. The variable md corresponds to the post-extraction weight of the dried sample post-extraction, whereas \xadmiso denotes the isolated weight of the sample before the extraction process.', 'The self-healing capability of PU-DA was qualitatively assessed using a Hirox KH-7700 digital microscope to observe the mending process of cracks induced by blades on the sample surfaces. The sheet samples were intentionally scratched at a depth of 0.5 mm and then subjected to five dif-ferent heating treatments to observe their self-healing ability. The treatments involved heating at temperatures of 100 °C, 110 °C, 120 °C, and 130 °C for 10 min, heating at 130 °C for 2 h, and cooling at 70 °C for 24 h. A quantitative study was conducted to evaluate the recovery of tensile strength following the self-healing process, utilizing a universal test-ing machine (Shimadzu AG–X Plus 50 kN). PU-DA samples were prepared as specimens according to ASTM D638 type 4, which were scratched in the center with a knife blade measuring 1 cm in length and 1 mm in depth. The self-healing process was then performed by heating the samples at 130 °C for 2 h, followed by a 24-h period at 70 °C. The self-healing efficiency was calculated based on the recovery of tensile strength using Eq. (3)', 'Healing Efficiency (% ) = Tensile strength healedTensile strength original × 100%. The synthesis pathway of PU-DA is depicted in Fig. 1, and the reaction conditions are explained in Table 1. Initially, PEG was reacted with an excess PDI-trimer to obtain a prepolymer with isocyanates as the terminal functional groups. Because of the different reaction conditions applied during this step, the prepolymers were assumed to possess different properties, as observed during the reaction. The prepolymer was subsequently reacted with furfuryl amine (FA), resulting in the formation of furan-terminated polyurethane (PU-FA). The final product polyurethane Diels–Alder (PU-DA) was obtained through the Diels–Alder reaction between PU-FA and bismaleimide (BMI) at 80 °C, followed by solvent evaporation at 60 °C under vacuum. Structural analysis of polyethylene glycol (PEG), the PDI-trimer, PU-FA, and PU-DA was performed using 1H-NMR spectroscopy (Fig. 2).', '3 \x07Results and discussion', '3.1 \x07Synthesis of polyurethane Diels–Alder (PU‑DA)', 'The NMR spectrum of PU-FA (Fig. 2c) reveals the suc-cessful synthesis of the PU prepolymer and the subsequent incorporation of the furan group. The PU prepolymer struc-ture is revealed by the emergence of a carbamate bond (NH‒COO) peak at 3.41 ppm (c). The furan group attached to the prepolymer is distinguished by the presence of distinct furan ring peaks at 6.18 ppm (m), 6.37 ppm (n), and 7.55 ppm (o). Additionally, an amide group, which indicates the urethane or urea proton NH, was observed at 5.90 ppm (d, k). Further-more, the product of the PU-FA and BMI reactions (Fig. 2d), a Diels–Alder adduct, is characterized by notable proton shifts at 3.07 ppm (r), 5.18 ppm (q), and 6.42–6.59 ppm (p). The aromatic structure of BMI is indicated by peaks in the range of 7.26–7.35 ppm (s, t). Additionally, the characteris-tic methylene peak originating from BMI was identified at 3.9 ppm (u).', '@', '@', "The formation of the polyurethane prepolymer from the reaction between polyethylene glycol (PEG) and the pentamethylene diisocyanate (PDI) trimer was confirmed through FTIR spectroscopy, as depicted in Fig. 3 and S1. The FTIR spectrum reveals a characteristic absorption peak at 2270 cm⁻1, which corresponds to the stretching vibration of the isocyanate (NCO) group. This distinct peak serves as a definitive indicator of the presence of the polyurethane prepolymer and remains detectable throughout the progression of the reaction, providing consistent evidence of the NCO group's role in the synthesis process. The ATR-FTIR spectra shown in Fig. 3 for the PU prepolymer, PU-FA, and PU-DA support the findings of the 1H NMR analysis. The lack of a peak at 2270 cm⁻1 in PU-FA confirms that the isocyanate group has reacted, indicating no residual isocyanate group remains. In contrast, the emergence of peaks at 1012, 736, and 811 \xadcm−1 distinctly confirms the incorporation of a furan ring in PU–FA [31, 32]. Compared with PU-FA, PU-DA has an obvious shoulder peak at 1710 \xadcm−1, which is characteristic of the DA adduct (–C = O band of imide) [33]. Simultaneously, the intensity decreases at 1012, 811, and 736 \xadcm−1, stemming from the consumption of the furan ring by the BMI molecule (Fig. 3b). This reduction clearly indicates the success of the PU–FA reaction with BMI through the Diels–Alder reaction.", '@', '@ DSC analyses of both the pristine PEG and PU-DA formulations were carried out over a temperature range of 0 °C to 190 °C. Figure 4a shows the DSC curve of pristine PEG, which shows distinct melting and crystallization peaks at 61.1 °C and 21.5 °C, respectively. In contrast, Fig. 4b shows that the endothermic peak (melting temperature) of PU-DA is generally lower than that of pristine PEG (61.1 °C). This difference results from the hindrance of multiple PEG crystalline segments as they interact during the formation of Diels–Alder cross-links, restricting their ability to organize into a well-defined crystalline structure. [34]. The cooling DSC curve (Fig. 4c) shows the reformation of PEG characterized by an exothermic peak. Owing to the inhibition of chain mobility by Diels–Alder crosslinks, PU-DA formulations tend to display higher crystallization temperatures than pristine PEG, indicating that recrystallization occurred earlier during cooling. Figure 4d shows the first and second heating–cooling cycles in the DSC thermogram of PU-DA formula 6. In the first heating cycle (black line), the thermogram of PU shows a modest endothermic peak between 110 and 150 °C, indicating the occurrence of a retro-DA reaction (release of furan and maleimide group bonds) [35]. Moreover, in the cooling curve (red line), a small exothermic peak at approximately 72 °C (as clearly shown in the inset photograph) suggests', '3.2 \x07Thermal characteristics and reversibility of Diels–Alder (DA) bonds the reformation of DA adduct bonds, which are possibly obscured by PEG recrystallization. Meanwhile, during the second heating cycle (blue line), the absence of the endothermic peak can be attributed to the insufficient interval between the two cycles, which prevented the complete reconstruction of the DA bonds. This inadequacy led to the peak not being discernible by DSC during the second heating cycle [36, 37].', 'In Table 2, the melting enthalpies of the eight polyu-rethane Diels–Alder (PU-DA) formulations ranged from 25.5 to 134.5 J/g, while the crystallization enthalpies (ΔHc) ranged from 26.2 to 136.8 J/g. Compared to pristine PEG (ΔHm = 158.3 J/g, ΔHc = 137.0 J/g), the PU-DA formulation exhibits a lower melting and crystallization enthalpy, sug-gesting that the crystallization and melting of PEG segments are hindered. This reduction is attributed to the restricted molecular mobility imposed by Diels–Alder cross-linking, which limits the ability of PEG chains to organize into a well-defined crystalline structure [38]. The synthesis of PU-DA involved NCO/OH ratios of 1.5 and 3. Notably, the data in Table 2 indicate that an NCO/OH ratio of 3 resulted in lower melting and crystallization enthalpies than an NCO/OH ratio of 1.5. This difference may stem from the higher NCO/OH ratio, which intensifies the disruption of PEG crystalline segments as a result of increased Diels–Alder cross-link formation. Consequently, the restricted molecular mobility leads to lower melting and crystallization enthalp-ies compared to the sample with an NCO/OH ratio of 1.5. This is further evidenced by the NCO/OH ratio of 3, which corresponds to a higher gel content value compared to the ratio of 1.5.', 'One advantage of intrinsic self-healing materials lies in the repeatability of their healing process, which stems from the reversibility of dynamic bonds. The thermal reversibility of Diels–Alder bonds further elucidate this mechanism. The thermal treatment of the PU-DA samples (Fig. 5a) and subsequent DSC measurements with consistent parameters revealed significant insights. In Fig. 5b, the observed peak (38–60 °C) indicates the melting of PEG crystals within the Diels–Alder polyurethane matrix. The thermograms also revealed modest endothermic peaks, indicating the breakage of DA adducts from the original polymer synthesis', '@', '@ process (DA1) at 120 °C (sample F5-PUDA) and 140 °C (F6-PUDA). After heat treatment and cooling at 70 °C, DSC observations (Fig. 5c and Table S2) revealed decreased melting and crystallization enthalpies of PEG in DA2 compared with those in DA1, which was attributed to the presence of DA2 bonds hindering the mobility of the PEG crystalline segments [39]. The decrease in enthalpies indicates Diels–Alder bond reconstruction during heating at 70 °C for 24 h. This phenomenon confirmed the reproducible thermal reversibility of the synthesized PU-DA samples.', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', 'Tm and \xadTc represent the melting/crystallization temperature, respectively; ΔHm and ΔHc represent the melt-ing/crystallization enthalpy, respectively, and the density (ρ), degree of swelling (Q), and gel content (G) were measured experimentally', '@', '@', 'Figure 6 shows the TGA and DTG curves of the Diels–Alder polyurethane sample under consistent heating conditions. The DTG curve reveals two distinct stages of thermal decomposition, marked by a minor peak in the initial stage and a substantial mass loss in the subsequent stage. The observed minor peak at approximately 160 °C likely arises from the evaporation of residual DMF within the material [40]. While the TGA curves of the PU-DA series are similar, the polyurethane samples F2 and F8 exhibit lower thermal stability, which can be related to their lower gel contents. In this sample, the rapid formation of the PU prepolymer restricts the extent to which furan groups can react with the prepolymer. Consequently, furan groups face challenges in integrating into the maleimide structure, leading to decreased formation of Diels–Alder crosslinks.', '3.3 \x07Healing ability', 'The self-healing behaviors of the eight PU-DA samples were comprehensively assessed using qualitative and quantitative methodologies. The qualitative evaluation involved a scratch test to visually assess the material’s healing capacity, whereas quantitative analysis was performed by determining the percentage of healing efficiency via hardness measurements. To qualitatively explore the healing process, the PU-DA film samples were subjected to five distinct heating treatments. The four treatments included heating the samples at temperatures ranging from 100 to 130 °C for 10 min each, followed by cooling to ambient temperature. The fifth treatment consisted of heating the sample at 130 °C for 2 h to promote the retro-Diels–Alder reaction, followed by heating at 70 °C for 24 h to facilitate the Diels–Alder reaction. The outcomes were evaluated using a digital microscope set at 100 × magnification. The results of the four thermal treatments are shown in Figure S2-S3, and the results of the fifth thermal treatment are shown in Fig. 8.', '@', '@', 'Polyurethane chains consist of soft segments derived from polyols and hard segments derived from isocyanates, with thermal energy influencing the mobility of these segments. Generally, soft segments exhibit greater mobility than hard segments upon heating, while hard segments restrict the thermal motion of molecular chains. Consequently, the self-healing capability of PU-DA is significantly influenced by the ratio of soft-to-hard segments, which can be controlled by adjusting the NCO/OH molar ratio. Observations from optical microscopy (Fig. 7) of the healing test conducted at 100 °C indicate that samples with an NCO/OH ratio of 1.5 (formulas F1–F4) exhibit faster scratch recovery compared to those with a ratio of 3 (formulas F5–F8). Similar trends were observed at 110, 120, and 130 °C (Figure S2.I-II and S3.III-IV), where samples with an NCO/OH ratio of 1.5 demonstrated superior healing efficiency. This phenomenon can be attributed to the higher proportion of soft segments in the 1.5 ratio, which enhances the mobility of the PU chains, allowing PU-FA and BMI to reflow and fill cracks after heat treatment. Conversely, samples with an NCO/OH ratio of 3 contain fewer soft segments, restricting polymer chain mobility and impeding the healing process [41].', '@', '@', '@', '@', "The thermally induced self-healing property of PU-DA is linked to the dissociation and reformation of Diels–Alder (DA) bonds. The images below illustrate the response of the PU-DA samples to various temperature treatments, confirm-ing the reconstruction of the DA bonds in the crosslinked network under thermal stimulation. PU-DA film samples etched at the same position were heated at temperatures ranging from 100 to 130 °C for 10 min each. At 100 °C, most scratches remained visible, although slight healing at the scratch base was observed (Figure S2.I). At 110 °C, some PU-DA samples exhibited increased healing, although small scars from incomplete healing were still present (Fig-ure S2.II). Treatments at 120 °C and 130 °C (Figures S3. III-IV) resulted in significantly better healing responses than those at lower temperatures, with tightly closed cracks indi-cating a reversible reaction between the furan and maleimide groups on the PU-DA surface [34]. Wool and O'Connor’s theory posits that the polymer chain diffusion rate is cru-cial in controlling the healing speed during the crack heal-ing process [42]. The temperature significantly affected the diffusion rate. When heated, the scratched surface deforms back to its original position, bringing the separated surfaces into contact. Simultaneously, the retro-DA reaction acceler-ates chain interdiffusion between the crack surfaces after clo-sure, leading to successful healing [43]. The scratches on the PU-PA film disappeared completely after heating to 130 °C for 2 h (Figure S3). This heating caused fluidization, which facilitated self-healing of the damaged area. Upon cooling to 70 °C, rebonding occurred, leading to the reconstruction of the Diels–Alder cross-linked network [44].", 'The quantitative evaluation of self-healing was conducted by measuring the recovery of tensile strength using a univer-sal testing machine (Shimadzu AG–X Plus 50kN). PU-DA samples were prepared by casting polymer films from a DMF solution, with dimensions of 17 cm in length, 16 cm in width, and a thickness ranging from 0.5 to 1 mm. The films were then subjected to solvent evaporation at 65 °C for 24 h. Specimens were prepared by punching the films according to the ASTM D638 type 4 standard, followed by introducing a scratch measuring 1 cm in length and 1 mm in depth at the center using a knife blade. The self-healing process was performed by heating the specimens at 130 °C for 2 h, and then maintaining them at 70 °C for 24 h. The healing efficiency was quantified based on the recovery of tensile strength, calculated using Eq. (3).', 'In this study, the self-healing behavior of PU-DA samples was evaluated using formulations with an NCO/OH ratio of 3 for the F5-F8 samples and 1.5 for the F1-F4 samples. Figure 8b presents the representative stress–strain curves of the original and healed PU-DA samples after being scratched. The corresponding values of tensile strength, elongation at break, and elastic modulus are shown in Fig. 8c–8e. The results from the tensile strength and elongation at break tests (Fig. 8c and 8d) indicate a distinct trend in healing performance, samples with an NCO/OH ratio of 1.5 exhibited a reduction in tensile strength and elongation at break after healing, whereas those with an NCO/OH ratio of 3 showed an increase in both properties following the healing process. Furthermore, the elastic modulus (Fig. 8e) increased in all PU-DA samples, regardless of the NCO/OH ratio. The evaluation of self-healing performance in PU-DA samples revealed distinct differences in mechanical properties depending on the NCO/OH ratio used during synthesis. Specifically, PU-DA formulations with an NCO/OH ratio of 1.5 (Formulas F1–F5) exhibited a noticeable decline in tensile strength after the healing process when compared to PU-DA specimens that had not undergone thermal treatment. This reduction suggests that a lower cross-linking density may have limited the material’s ability to recover its original mechanical integrity. Conversely, PU-DA samples synthesized with an NCO/OH ratio of 3 (Formulas F5–F8) displayed a significant enhancement in healing efficiency, surpassing 100%. This remarkable self-healing capability is primarily attributed to the presence of a greater number of cross-links within the polymer network, which is directly correlated with the observed increase in gel content. The higher cross-link density not only facilitated more effective reformation of the molecular structure after damage but also contributed to improved tensile strength, exceeding that of the original PU-DA sample before healing. These findings highlight the critical role of cross-linking density in optimizing self-healing properties and mechanical robustness in thermally reversible polyurethane systems.', "The self-healing performance of PU-DA is governed by the synergistic effects of thermally induced polyurethane chain mobility and the thermo-reversible Diels–Alder (DA) reaction. Consequently, the self-healing capability of PU-DA is strongly influenced by the ratio of soft-to-hard segments, which can be controlled through the NCO/OH molar ratio. At an NCO/OH ratio of 1.5, the tensile strength after healing decreases compared to the original PU-DA sample (see Table 3). In contrast, when compared to a ratio of 3, both the pre- and post-healing tensile strength values are lower. This phenomenon can be attributed to the lower crosslink density, as indicated by the gel content, in PU-DA samples with an NCO/OH ratio of 1.5 compared to those with a ratio of 3 (see Fig. 9f and Table S3). The reduced crosslink density in the 1.5 ratio results in lower tensile strength relative to the 3.0 ratio. At a higher hard-segment content (i.e., an NCO/OH ratio of 3), the formation of additional crosslinks between hard segments enhances structural integrity, requiring more energy to disrupt the network [45]. Self-healing efficiency is influenced by the polymer chain's ability to diffuse across the crack area and the availability of broken cross-links for reformation. These two factors exhibit an inverse relationship: as the cross-link density increases, chain diffusion is hindered, leading to a higher number of broken cross-links. At lower cross-linking levels, mechanical strength decreases. This phenomenon explains why the self-healing efficiency of the PU-DA sample with an NCO/OH ratio of 1.5 is lower compared to the ratio of 3, where the gel content is higher. Additionally, self-healing values exceeding 100%, which are attributed to the presence of cross-links reflected in the gel content, result in higher tensile strength than the original PU-DA sample [46, 47].", '4 \x07Conclusions', 'The behavior of the isocyanate trimer (PDI-trimer) in the synthesis of self-healing polyurethane (PU-DA) via the Diels–Alder (DA) reaction was investigated through the reaction between the furan-terminated prepolymer and bismaleimide. Digital microscopy and DSC observations revealed that cracks with a depth of 0.5 mm in the PU-DA film could be repaired after heat treatment at 120–130 °C for 10 min, achieving complete healing at 130 °C for 10 min, followed by cooling at 70 °C for 24 h. The tightly closed cracks indicate a reversible reaction between the furan and maleimide groups on the surface of the PU-DA sample. Additionally, the DSC results revealed an endothermic peak in the PU-DA material (DA1), indicating the breakage of the Diels–Alder bond (r-DA). After cooling at 70 °C for 24 h, the endothermic peak remained visible (DA2), demonstrating the thermal reversibility of the synthesized PU-DA samples, which allows for repeated healing due to the reversibility of the Diels–Alder bond.', '@', '@', 'a A self-healing efficiency of 100% is attained when a PU-DA sample that undergoes a healing process—comprising heat treatment at 130 °C for 2 h followed by additional heating at 70 °C for 24 h—restores its tensile strength to the same level as the original, unhealed sample that has not undergone any thermal treatment. It is important to note that PU-DA1 refers to the pristine PU-DA sample, while PU-DA2 represents the healed PU-DA specimen', '@', '@', 'The NCO/OH ratio significantly influences the forma-tion of the furan‒maleimide crosslink bond structure. At a ratio of 3, the formation of PEG crystals is smaller than that at a ratio of 1.5, indicating that a higher ratio leads to more crosslink bonds and a decrease in the melting enthalpy value in DSC measurements. The healing efficiency of PU-DA samples with an NCO/OH ratio of 1.5 (Formulas F1-F5) showed a reduction in tensile strength compared to the unheated PU-DA specimens. In contrast, PU-DA sam-ples with an NCO/OH ratio of 3 (Formulas F5-F8) dem-onstrated enhanced healing efficiency, exceeding 100%, which can be attributed to the higher density of cross-links. This increased cross-linking, as reflected in the gel content, resulted in greater tensile strength than the original PU-DA sample. These findings provide valuable insights into the development of self-healing materials utilizing PDI-trimer via the Diels–Alder reaction, a relatively underexplored area in polymer research.', 'Supplementary Information The online version containssupplementary material available at https://\u200bdoi.\u200borg/\u200b10.\u200b1007/s13233-\u200b025-\u200b00400-y.', 'Acknowledgements This research was supported by the ResearchOrganization for Nanotechnology and Materials [National Researchand Innovation Agency of Indonesia (No. 3/III.10/HK/2023)] and theResearch Center for Polymer Technology.', 'Author contributions All authors contributed to the study conception and design. 2025-05-20 14:43:24 - WARNING: ['Mechanistic investigation of the influence sulfur curing systems on the characteristics of chlorosulfonated  polyethylene rubber', 'Hossein Roshanaei1 · Mohammad Reza Pourhossaini1  · Mahmoud Razavizadeh1 · Mohammad Khabiri1 · Hassan Fattahi1', 'Received: 14 January 2025 / Revised: 21 March 2025 / Accepted: 30 March 2025  © The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer-Verlag GmbH Germany, part of Springer Nature 2025', 'Abstract', 'This study systematically investigates new insights into how sulfur-based curing agents influence the properties of chlorosulfonated polyethylene (CSM) rubber in the presence of magnesium oxide and pentaerythritol. The rheological, thermal, and cross-linking properties of CSM rubber under various sulfur curing processes are used to correlates its curing behavior with the network characteristics. Employing advanced analytical techniques such as rheometery, differential scanning calorimetry, and attenuated total reflectance Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy, the formulations were evaluated containing magnesium oxide (MgO), pentaerythritol, sulfur, and accelerators like TMTD and MBTS. Among the studied formulations, the combination of pentaerythritol, MBTS, TMTD, and sulfur exhibited the most favorable curing dynamics, achieving the highest curing enthalpy (8.1 J/g) and vulcanization rate (0.42 dNm/min) while maintaining the lowest loss factor (tan δ = 0.07). The findings demonstrate a direct correlation between an enhanced cross-linking network and improved thermal stability, with the dehydrochlorination temperature reaching a peak value of 259 °C. Notably, the results highlight the dominance of metal oxide crosslink in the sulfur-free compound. Additionally, the analysis demonstrates that MgO and pentaerythritol cannot form active complexes and enhance the curing quality without the aid of accelerator and sulfur. These insights provide a comprehensive understanding of sulfur curing systems for CSM rubber and suggest pathways for tailoring its thermal and curing properties. The findings hold significant potential for advancing materials design in high-performance rubber applications.', 'Graphical abstract', '@', 'Keywords Chlorosulfonated polyethylene (CSM) rubber · Sulfur vulcanization · Rheological characteristics · Mechanism', 'Introduction', 'The synthesis of chlorosulfonated polyethylene (CSM) involves the chlorination and sulfochlorination of low-density polyethylene (LDPE) or high-density polyethylene (HDPE) [1]. In general, polyethylene with an approximate number average molecular weight of 20,000 is used to produce CSM. Typically, the polyethylene possesses one elongated branch for every 100 carbon atoms in the primary chain and two to four small branches for approximately every four carbon atoms. At a chlorine content of 30%, there are around 17 chlorine atoms for every 100 carbon atoms in the backbone [2, 3]. The physical and mechanical properties of rubber products vary based on cross-linking efficiency, which affects the performance of the product [4]. CSM undergoes vulcanization, and its saturated backbone enhances its resistance to heat, weathering, chemicals, and oil [5–7].', 'While researchers have extensively investigated CSM rubber curing, they have not thoroughly examined the sulfur curing mechanism of this rubber [8, 9]. Additionally, numerous methodologies have been devised to investigate the thermal properties and phase structure of CSM rubber [10, 11]. Nanda and Seleznev [4, 12] have extensively examined the curing mechanisms of CSM rubber, specifically discussing ionic and covalent curing in their research. Nanda has investigated four curing systems for CSM rubber: epoxy, metal oxide, peroxide, and sulfur, and subsequently proposed a distinct mechanism for each. Nersasian and Andersen [13] examined the physical and mechanical properties of chlorosulfonated polyethylene (CSM) vulcanizates cured using a system based on magnesium oxide (MgO), pentaerythritol, and accelerators, along with titanium compounds. Their findings revealed that this curing approach formed dense ionic crosslinked networks, leading to improved tensile strength, elasticity, and thermal stability.', 'CSM rubber provides two curing methods: ionic curing and covalent curing. Ionic curing involves moisture-accelerated ionic cross-linking and a divalent metal oxide acid acceptor. Covalent curing entails covalent cross-linking, frequently employed for CSM rubber [14–16]. Researchers always face the difficulty of adapting the curing technique for rubber composites to their individual applications. Therefore, their study focuses on using the suitable vulcanization agent in relation to precise values [17–20]. Roychoudhury et al. assessed the self-cross-linking characteristics of CSM and the chemical interactions between CSM and silica [21–23].', 'Sulfur vulcanizing agents can be used in conjunction with other activators or accelerators to enhance the desired physical properties, process safety, and curing rate [3]. Organic sulfur compounds, including accelerators and sulfur donors, are also parts of realistic vulcanization systems. In the presence of metal oxides, they generate covalent crosslinks with active chloride structures, which may involve sulfur [2]. Primary sulfur vulcanizing agents, accelerators that contain sulfur, peroxides (often in combination with coagents), and maleimides like N,N′-m-phenylene-dimaleimide (HVA-2). Various accelerators and coagents are used to accelerate the activation of the acid acceptor or the cross-linking activity of the primary curing agent. Sulfur and sulfur donors, such as TMTD, TETD, and DPTT, provide safe processing, low cost, and good overall properties as curing agents for both light and dark-colored CSM [5]. Mastromateo et al. [24] indicated that employing accelerators with greater solubility in CSM rubber can enhance curing compatibility.', 'Typically, cross-linking agents and activators are employed in conjunction with magnesium, lithium, and other substances to achieve an appropriate state of cure and curing rates. Acid acceptors fulfill two functions: They neutralize acids generated during vulcanization and serve as cross-linking agents [5]. Alam et al. [25] describe the interactions between zinc-based and non-zinc-based cross-linking accelerators in conjunction with MgO as a curing activator, aiming to identify an alternative to ZnO, an environmental pollutant, in the vulcanization of natural rubber. Therefore, in the absence of zinc oxide, vulcanization can fully utilize MgO, a more readily available and nontoxic alternative. They also found that MgO as a co-cure activator improved rubber vulcanization curing kinetics [26–28].', 'Pentaerythritol serves as a curing accelerator alongside magnesium, improving the curing rate and increasing the state of curing (SOC). The primary polyols solely function as activators and necessitate the presence of sulfur, sulfur donors, or alternative curing agents. Polyols can interact with litharge acid receptors, but the resulting scorch time is extremely short. These also increase water absorption. Experience indicates that pentaerythritol offers the optimal equilibrium among process safety, cost, and vulcanization characteristics [5, 13, 29].', 'This study offers a detailed sulfur curing mechanism of CSM rubber and investigates the interaction between magnesium oxide and pentaerythritol in forming ionic crosslinks and their thermal properties. Techniques such as rheometery, ATR-FTIR, and DSC were employed to elucidate some details of the sulfur vulcanization of CSM rubber by focusing on four distinct curing systems that incorporate varying combinations of magnesium oxide, pentaerythritol, sulfur, and accelerators. The formulations, which range from systems dominated by magnesium oxide to those containing pentaerythritol and traditional accelerators, enable a strategic evaluation of the individual contributions of each component. These findings pave the way for optimizing high-performance elastomeric formulations.', 'Experimental', 'Materials and methods', 'The chlorosulfonated polyethylene rubber (TS-530 brand) used in this study was supplied by TOSOH Co. (Japan), containing 35% chlorine and 1% sulfur (Table 1). Magnesium oxide (MAGOX Super Premium, USA) with a mesh size of 325 was provided by Premier Magnesia Co. Pentaerythritol (> 98% monomeric, 200 mesh) was sourced from J&H Chem Co. (China). The curing accelerators, 2-mercaptobenzothiazole disulfide (MBTS) and tetramethylthiuram disulfide (TMTD), were obtained from Shandong Sunsine Co. and Dingyuan Chemical Co. (China), respectively. The sulfur curing agent was procured from Partogoogerd Co. (Iran). Additional information about these additives is detailed in Table 2.', 'Subsequently, compounds were formulated and encoded according to Table 3 (all values are presented in phr) to investigate the influence of modifying different systems on the curing process of the compounds. Pentaerythritol, MBTS, and sulfur were incorporated as variables in these compounds, while MgO and TMTD were maintained as constants throughout the study.', 'The mixing process utilized the Brabender internal mixer, manufactured by the Polymer and Petrochemical Institute of Iran. The fill factor was 0.75, and the rotor speed was 60 rpm. The mixing temperature commenced at 70 °C, while the discharge temperature was maintained at 90 ± 5 °C. Following a 12-h period of rest, each blend was processed five times using the German-manufactured laboratory two-roll mill, the Schweanthan Polymix 200L. All blends were prepared utilizing the identical procedure, as detailed in Table 4.', 'Methods for characterization', 'Diverse standardized instruments and techniques were employed for the preparation,curing, and measurement of the samples.', 'Curing and viscoelastic characteristics', 'The MDR 3000 Pro from the German company MonTech was utilized for testing curing time and kinetics. The ASTM D5289 standard, analogous to the ISO 6502 standard, was employed to perform this test. This test was conducted at temperatures ranging from 150 to 170 °C, a frequency of 1.67 Hz, and a strain of 7%, corresponding to 0.5 degrees. The MDR rheometric test is used to determine the state of cure, optimal curing time, and scorch time. In order to determine rheometric mechanical properties like minimum torque ML, maximum torque MH, and torque increment (ΔM), calculated as (MH − ML), as well as kinetic parameters like scorch time TS2 (which takes into account a 2% increase in torque difference) and optimal curing time T90 (which takes into account a 90% increase in torque difference), this tool offers a torque versus time graph at a constant vulcanization temperature. The rate of vulcanization (RV) and the curing rate index (CRI) are derived from Eqs. 1 and 2, respectively [30–33]:', '@', '@', 'RV = (MT90−MTs2)∕(T90−Ts2)', 'CRI = 100 ∕(T90−Ts2)', 'The tool can also compute dynamic properties including shear modulus, loss factor (tan δ), and viscosity. The test was based on a time sweep at constant temperature, and the dynamic properties were reported at the time of maximum torque (MH) occurrence. Energy is dissipated by the rubber during impact. Characteristics that delineate the energy dissipation capability of the material are referred to as dynamic properties. The complex modulus G* is a complex quantity that can be separated into a real component, known as the storage modulus G′, and an imaginary component, referred to as the loss modulus G″. Their relationship is depicted in Eq. 3 [34].', 'G* = G�+ i G��', 'The storage modulus G′ indicates a material’s ability to store and release energy to the environment, while the loss modulus G″ reflects a material’s capacity to dissipate energy within its internal structure. An additional significant parameter derived from the dynamic time sweep test is tan δ. The loss factor tan δ, defined as the ratio of the loss or viscous modulus to the storage or elastic modulus, is associated with molecular motion [32, 35–37]. Equation 4 delineates the methodology for calculating the loss factor.', 'tan 훿= G��∕G�', 'The structure of the rubber is directly related to the parameter known as complex viscosity (η*). This parameter indicates the extent of crosslink formation or degradation [38]. η* demonstrates Power law behavior and is computed as a function of angular frequency. The determination of complex viscosity relates to Eq. 5 [39]:', '@', '@', '@', '@', '휂∗= K 휔n−1', 'where ω denotes the frequency, and K and n are empirical constants (n < 1). The constant K in Eq. 5 denotes the quantification of flow resistance.', 'Differential scanning calorimetry (DSC)', 'A thermal evaluation of the compounds and specific raw materials was conducted under an inert gas atmosphere using a differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) apparatus. The analysis utilized a Netzsch DSC 200-F3 instrument (Germany), operated at a consistent heating rate of 10 °C/min over a temperature range of  −  100 to 300 °C. The heat flow difference between the sample pan containing the specimen and the empty reference pan was recorded as the temperature increased, generating DSC thermograms for analysis.', 'To ensure reliability and consistency, each heating cycle was performed under identical experimental conditions, with fresh samples of similar mass used for each run. The heat flow variations, recorded under a heating rate of 10 °C/min, were meticulously monitored and visualized as DSC thermograms. This test provided critical parameters such as the glass transition temperature (Tg), melting temperature (Tm), crystallization temperature (Tc), curing enthalpy (ΔHc), and dehydrochlorination temperature. These values were accurately determined using the Proteus analysis software integrated with the instrument, ensuring precisecharacterization of the thermal properties.', '@', '@', 'Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy—attenuated total reflectance (ATR‑FTIR)', 'The functional groups present in the compounds were analyzed using ATR-FTIR spectroscopy. A BRUKER TENSOR 27 spectrometer (Germany) was employed for this purpose, operating within the spectral range of 600–4000 cm⁻1. The objective of the analysis was to identify the characteristic absorption bands that correspond to the functional groups in the rubber matrix and additives. Chemical changes or interactions that resulted from the curing process were detected using the recorded spectra, which provided insight into the structural and compositional properties of the compounds.', 'Result and discussion', 'Curing and viscoelastic properties', 'The rheometric test for various CSM rubber compounds at temperatures ranging from 150 to 170 °C yielded torque variations over time represented as a curve. In this section’s analysis, only the curves derived from Fig. 1 at a temperature of 160 °C were utilized. The rheometric curve was limited to a duration of 100 min because the MTS1 compound exhibited the marching modulus phenomenon in the absence of pentaerythritol, allowing it to attain its maximum torque within this timeframe. The data obtained from this curve encompass maximum and minimum torques, scorch time, state of cure (SOC), ΔM, CRI, and RV, which are essential for ascertaining the curing parameters, as depicted in Fig. 2 and summarized in Table 5.', 'Table 5 indicates that the variations in torque, scorch time, and curing time differ for each compound. The scorch time TS2 is defined as the moment when the curing torque rises by two units relative to the minimum torque. This period, which is a function of temperature and comes just after the induction time, is when the rubber only experiences a physical change due to heating rather than any chemical change. Some references consider the induction time when the torque increases by one unit over the minimum torque [40–42]. The subsequent analysis examines the influence of temperature and blend content on curing behavior.', 'In Fig. 2a, the lack of pentaerythritol curing activator results in MTS1 exhibiting the longest scorch time (5.3 min), whereas the absence of the MBTS accelerator, due to its time delay, leads to PTS1 having the shortest scorch time (2.6 min) in comparison to PMTS1.', 'Figure 2b indicates that the MTS1 sample exhibits the longest curing time T90 at 42.4 min, attributable to the lack of pentaerythritol, while the PMTS0 sample demonstrates the shortest curing time at 15.0 min. The absence of sulfur in the compound, coupled with the presence of the accelerator TMTD, which functions as both a sulfur donor and an accelerator, eliminates the necessity for the accelerator to open the S8 ring during the curing reaction, resulting in a reduced curing time.', 'Figure 2c indicates that the MTS1 sample exhibits the highest torque of 9.6 (dNm), while the PMTS0 sample demonstrates the lowest torque of 5.2 (dNm). Figure 2d demonstrates that the highest torque increment, calculated from the difference between maximum and minimum torque (MH − ML), is associated with MTS1, yielding a value of 8.9 (dNm). This was achieved after approximately 100 min for this compound. The predominant portion is ascribed to the marching modulus of this compound. In order to prevent this phenomenon, as well as to ensure a flat curing rate and an appropriate state of cure for the compounds, major accelerators such as TMTD and the Thiuram family are typically important in controlling the marching of CSM rubber. An auxiliary accelerator such as MBTS, in conjunction with TMTD and sulfur, improves the safety of scorching and provides an optimal curing condition for CSM rubbers [3]. In contrast, the PMTS0 sample exhibits the lowest torque increment value of 4.5 (dNm), due to the lack of sulfur. The inclusion of TMTD, comprising roughly 13.3% active sulfur, facilitates ideal curing conditions for this compound. Sulfur donors may be utilized when a significant amount of sulfur cannot be incorporated into the compound. These materials are employed in high-temperature rubber vulcanization and in efficient (EV) and semi-efficient (SEV) vulcanization processes. Sulfur donors are utilized to establish a cross-linking network that resists degradation from thermal exposure. Typically, sulfur donors convert the initially formed polysulfide linkages into monosulfide, signifying EV or SEV systems [43]. Figure 2e reveals that the MTS1 sample has the lowest curing rate index at 2.7 (1/min), whereas the PMTS0 sample shows the highest at 8.6 (1/min). This phenomenon results from the deficiency of sulfur in the compound and the presence of TMTD and MBTS; consequently, the accelerator does not require the opening of the S8 ring during the curing reaction, leading to a higher curing rate index relative to other compounds. The rate of vulcanization, quantified in torque per minute, reflects the amount of cross-linking produced per time unit [44]. This measure simultaneously evaluates the degree of crosslink formation and the curing rate, thereby providing more accurate insights into the state of curing. With a value of 0.42 (dNm/min), the PMTS1 compound shows the highest rate of vulcanization formation, as shown in Fig. 2f. This compound’s curing efficiency is increased by the presence of sulfur and all of the curing ingredients, such as MBTS, TMTD accelerators, and pentaerythritol curing activator. The MTS1 compound has a higher cross-linking than the others, but because of its low CRI, it has a lower rate of vulcanization of 0.24 (dNm/min). The main cause of this problem is the lack of pentaerythritol as a curing activator.', 'Figure 3a illustrates the results for tan δ as a function of time in relation to the variations in the complex shear modulus (G*), while Fig. 3b depicts the results against the changes in complex viscosity (η*). The tan δ curve exhibits three distinct stages of the curing process. As is evident, the initial cross-linking reaction of the compounds in the induction time results in a maximum value of tan δ, which suggests that the material is softening and that there is no chemical reaction between the vulcanizing agents, rubber, and other components. The peak of tan δ can be associated with the point at which the flowability of the compounds reaches its maximum while the complex viscosity is at its lowest (Fig. 3b). No chemical reaction has occurred between the curing agents and rubber at this stage. During the curing stage, tan δ decreases rapidly due to the start of vulcanization and the formation of crosslinks. This results in a decrease in the loss shear modulus and a rapid increase in the storage shear modulus. The complex shear modulus reflects the sum of these changes and reaches its maximum in an inverse trend with tan δ (Fig. 3a).', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', 'The optimal curing time (T90) is achieved at the conclusion of this stage. A maximum plateau is typically observed in the final stage of vulcanization, which is accompanied by torque variations with time, as obtained by MDR. The identical plateau is distinctly observable in the tan δ curve; however, tan δ stabilizes at a minimum value over time.', 'Figure 4a illustrates that the mixed shear modulus (G*) reaches its peak value of 742.0 kPa at maximum torque (MH) for the MTS1 sample, attributable to the marching modulus phenomenon and the previously detailed cases. Also, the lowest value of G* with a value of 406.6 kPa corresponds to the PMTS0 sample. This signifies that this compound possesses the lowest modulus among the compounds and consequently lacks high strength properties. The complex shear modulus (G*) is directly related to and dependent on the crosslink network, in addition to its relationship with physical–mechanical properties.', 'Changes in complex viscosity are markedly affected by torque behavior, and as the curing torque increases, the η* value also rises. This parameter is highly compatible with the formation of crosslinks; a higher η* value is associated with a greater number of crosslinks in the cured compound. Figure 4b illustrates that the MTS1 sample exhibits the maximum complex viscosity at 70,715.2 (Pa.sec),whereas the PMTS0 sample has the lowest complex viscosity at 38,752.5 (Pa.sec).', 'The PMTS0 sample has the highest tan δ value, measuring 0.18, as shown in Fig. 4c. This arises from insufficient curing of the compound and its heightened loss modulus (G″) in comparison to the storage modulus (G′). A loss or viscous term that is higher and a storage or elastic term that is smaller are the consequences of a weaker crosslink network. Therefore, the loss factor tan δ is elevated. These two compounds, PMTS1 and MTS1, exhibit the lowest loss factor of 0.07. The reason for this is the establishment of a more robust cross-linking network and the completion of an adequate curing process in comparison to other compounds.', 'DSC results', 'This section examines the thermal analysis results of compounds subjected to a heating rate of 10 ℃/min in an inert nitrogen gas atmosphere. The chemical reactions of compounds and phase transitions in certain raw materials, notably CSM rubber, have also been examined.', 'Janowska et al. [16] demonstrated that among chlorosulfonated polyethylene, only the rubber with the lowest chlorine content (approximately 24%) possesses a crystalline phase that significantly influences its glass transition temperature. The maximum thermal degradation rate also decreases as the chlorine content in the heated sample increases. Chlorosulfonated polyethylene is a specialized rubber created through the modification and functionalization of polyethylene (PE) via the concurrent application of chlorine and sulfur dioxide on polyethylene (typically LDPE) dissolved in carbon tetrachloride \xad(CCl4) in the presence of radical initiators. There is 25 to 45 wt% chlorine and 1 to 2 wt% sulfur in commercial products.', 'On average, one chlorine atom is associated with 5–7 carbon atoms, but a single chlorosulfonyl group (–SO2Cl) is associated with 80–100 carbon atoms in the main chain of CSM. Over 90% of CSM chlorine is present in the secondary groups \xad(R1R2CHCl), up to 3.5% in the tertiary groups \xad(RR1R2CCl), and up to 3% in the \xadRCH2Cl groups, including the terminal groups, and the residual chlorine located in the chlorosulfonyl groups. The functional and adhesive capabilities, along with the chemical reaction of CSM, are dependent upon the quantity of Cl and S included [16]. According to the analysis of TGA thermogravimetric curves, Janowska et al. asserted that the thermal decomposition of CSM occurs in three stages. At 180 °C, the samples exhibit mass loss due to hydrogen chloride evolution, evidenced by a minor endothermic shift in the differential thermal analysis (DTA) curves at 210 °C. The initial temperature for the thermal decomposition of elastomers is independent of the chlorine content in the CSM. The subsequent phase is characterized by a significant release of HCl, commencing at a temperature of 250 °C. An increase in the chlorine content of the CSM is accompanied by a clear increase in the rate of this process and a decrease in the sample residue following the termination of heating. The amount of chlorine and sulfur in the polymers decreases significantly in the third stage after the CSM is heated to 320 °C. Thermogravimetric (TG) analysis and derived thermogravimetry (DTG) indicate that HCl evolution may occur via a chain mechanism, resulting in the formation of –CH=CH– CH=CH– double bonds [7, 16].', '@', '@', 'To validate and correlate the information acquired with the research referenced, the DSC test was performed on the CSM rubber utilized (TS-530). The rubber’s behavior is verified by the results of this test. The endothermic curve is undergoing the release of chlorine at a temperature of approximately 178.6 °C, as illustrated in Fig. 5a. Subsequently, the curve undergoes a significant decrease at 242.9 °C as a result of the release of HCl. At a temperature of 328.4 °C, a pronounced endothermic peak is detected, attributed to the substantial decrease of chlorine and sulfur in the rubber. The peak of chlorine loss at approximately 180 °C coincides with the vulcanization temperature range of the compounds. The selection of the appropriate curing temperature and time is crucial for sulfur curing, as the formation of sulfide crosslinks necessitates the separation of the chlorosulfonyl group and the subsequent release of chlorine attached to it. This is a result of the rubber curing process being coordinated with the release of chlorine. Figure 5b shows that due to the presence of pentaerythritol, all samples exhibit a prominent endothermic peak, with the exception of the MTS1 sample.', 'The heat of crystal transition of pentaerythritol is equivalent to the heat of fusion of ice. The spherical molecule of this substance transitions to a plastic crystalline state by rotating around its crystal site and breaking the intermolecular hydrogen bonds without migrating into the crystal at a specific temperature. The distinctive solid-to-solid transition defines pentaerythritol. The crystallization temperature of pentaerythritol is approximately 188 °C. Two endothermic peaks are detected in the analysis of pentaerythritol. The initial peak indicates the phase transition of pentaerythritol from a tetragonal to a cubic crystal structure at approximately 196 °C, while the subsequent broad peak at around 281.5 °C signifies its melting temperature [45].', 'To examine this phenomenon, it was essential to perform a DSC test on the pentaerythritol utilized in the compounds independently. Figure 5c illustrates a pronounced endothermic peak in this material, which complicates the analysis of the curing of chlorosulfonated polyethylene rubbers due to its overlap with the temperature range of the endothermic peak associated with chlorine release in CSM rubber and the exothermic peak of the compounds’ curing process. At a temperature of 193.1 °C, an endothermic peak indicative of the solid–solid crystalline phase transition from tetragonal to cubic is observed, alongside a melting endothermic peak (Tm) at 268.6 °C, as indicated by the research data.', '@', '@', 'Table 6 indicates that only the MTS1 sample, devoid of pentaerythritol, exhibits a peak curing temperature (Tc). Due to the endothermic peak associated with the crystalline phase change (Tp) of pentaerythritol, the peak curing of other compounds is not possible to determine. This is due to the phase transition occurring within the curing temperature range (ΔTc) of the samples. The broadest curing temperature range is exhibited by PMTS1 at 56 °C, succeeded by PTS1 at 55 °C, MTS1 at 37 °C, and PMTS0 at 36 °C, respectively. The PMTS1 sample has the highest curing temperature range. This compound offers an expanded curing range due to the inclusion of all curing components, such as activators, accelerators, and sulfur curing agents. The reduced temperature range of PTS1 relative to PMTS1 can solely be attributed to the lack of MBTS in the formulation. The other two compounds, having a shorter curing time than the previously mentioned compounds, exhibit a reduced curing temperature range and are consequently less robust.', 'As illustrated in Fig. 6a, the glass transition temperatures \xadTg of nearly all compounds are comparable, and as anticipated, there is no substantial distinction among them.', 'According to Fig. 6b, the maximum enthalpy of curing is for the PMTS1 sample with a value of 8.1 (J/g), and the lowest is for MTS1 with a value of 1.5 (J/g). The formation of a larger crosslink network and the proper curing of PMTS1 may be the reasons for its higher enthalpy of curing. The reduced enthalpy of curing for the PMTS0 compound may result from the absence of sulfur and the establishment of a less robust crosslink network. The presence of a pronounced pentaerythritol crystallinity peak hinders a more precise measurement of the enthalpy of curing. The low enthalpy of curing for the MTS1 sample results from its sluggish and feeble curing process, attributable to the lack of the pentaerythritol curing activator. The findings demonstrate a significant contribution of pentaerythritol, sulfur, and MBTS accelerator to the enhancement of curing enthalpy.', 'Figure 6c indicates that the peak dehydrochlorination or HCl release initiation temperature is associated with PTS1 at 259 °C, succeeded by PMTS1, PMTS0, and MTS1 mixtures at 256, 250, and 239 °C, respectively. This transition can indicate the type and quantity of crosslinks formed in the compounds. The formation of HCl can be shifted to higher temperatures as a result of the higher dissociation energy and the tendency toward mono- and disulfide due to the increased crosslinks. Ultimately, this transition has the potential to increase the temperature of rubber degradation. The quantity and quality of crosslinks are significantly influenced by the presence of sulfur in the mixtures and pentaerythritol, which serves as an activator of the curing system.', 'FTIR analysis', 'In Fig. 7a, the spectrum of CSM rubber shows two peaks at 1368 and 1159 \xadcm−1, which are respectively related to the asymmetric and symmetric stretching vibration of \xadSO2, indicating the presence of the \xadSO2Cl group. The peaks at 2924 and 2853 \xadcm−1 correspond to C-H stretching vibration in CSM, while the peaks at 802, 725, and 667 \xadcm−1 are attributed to C–Cl stretching vibration. The peak at 1459 \xadcm−1 is associated with C-H bending [3, 4, 8, 21, 46]. In Fig. 7b, the peak at 1180 \xadcm−1, associated with the C-S-C bond, signifies the occurrence of sulfur cross-linking during the curing process of the MTS1 sample. The reduction in intensity of the peak at 1364 \xadcm−1, associated with \xadSO2 stretching, and the absence of the peak at 1159 \xadcm−1 signify the detachment of the sulfonyl chloride group from the main chain, resulting in the emergence of the peak at 1614 \xadcm−1, indicative of C=C stretching. In Fig. 7c, the peak at a wavenumber of 1184 \xadcm−1 associated with the C-S-C bond signifies the occurrence of sulfur cross-linking during the curing process of the PTS1 sample. Additionally, the sulfonyl chloride group’s separation from the main chain is indicated by the decrease in intensity of the peak at 1372 \xadcm−1 corresponding to \xadSO2Cl and the disappearance of the peak at 1159 \xadcm−1. This separation results in the formation of the peak at 1614 \xadcm−1 corresponding to the C=C stretching.', 'Furthermore, Fig. 7d exhibits a peak at a wavenumber of 1184 \xadcm−1, which corresponds to the C-S-C bond, suggests that sulfur cross-linking occurred during the curing of the PMTS0 sample. The reduction in intensity of the peak associated with \xadSO2Cl at 1376 \xadcm−1 and the absence of the peak at 1159 \xadcm−1 signify the detachment of the sulfonyl chloride group from the main chain, resulting in the emergence of the peak at 1622 \xadcm−1 corresponding to C=C stretching. The two sulfonate geminal peaks \xad(SO2–O–SO2) at 1543 and 1580 \xadcm−1 signify the ionic bond formed by the cross-linking of the metal oxide. In the absence of sulfur in this compound, some cross-linking is of the metal oxide variety (Scheme 1), while the remainder is of the sulfide type due to the presence of the sulfur donor [4, 12].', '@', '@', 'Figure 7e illustrates that the peak at a wavenumber of 1184 \xadcm−1, associated with the C–S–C bond, signifies the occurrence of sulfur cross-linking during the curing process of the PMTS1 sample. The reduction in intensity of the peak at 1372 \xadcm−1 associated with \xadSO2Cl and the absence of the peak at 1159 \xadcm−1 signify the detachment of the sulfonyl chloride group from the main chain, resulting in theemergence of the peak at 1610 \xadcm−1 corresponding to C=C stretching.', '@', '@', 'Suggested CSM rubber curing mechanism', 'Typically, in the majority of rubber curing processes, ZnO serves as an activator in conjunction with stearic acid, which forms a complex with accelerators, thereby generating an active species for interaction with both sulfur and the rubber chain [47–49]. In accordance with Guzman et al. [50], MgO accelerates the decomposition of the accelerator; however, ZnO produces a greater degree of cross-linking. Using CBS as an accelerator, Sánchez [51] discovered that when ZnO and MgO were mixed in NR, MgO cured with better kinetic than ZnO, but the resulting cross-linking density was significantly lower. Because MgO can generate active sulfurating agent more quickly than ZnO, it seems to have a higher affinity for sulfur. Nevertheless, MgO intermediates were unable to facilitate final cross-linking to the same extent as ZnO-activated sulfurating agents [28, 50]. Pentaerythritol enhances the cross-linking process by reacting with the acidic sites of the CSM rubber, improving the network formation during curing. CSM rubber contains chlorine and sulfonic acid groups, which can produce acidic by-products during processing and curing. These acidic species can hinder the curing process and degrade the material [52–54]. MgO acts as a primary acid scavenger by neutralizing these acidic by-products, forming magnesium salts. Pentaerythritol complements MgO by interacting with these acidic species and stabilizing the curing environment. Together, they ensure a consistent and effective vulcanization process [55]. Pentaerythritol enhances the reactivity of MgO by acting as a dispersing agent, ensuring MgO is evenly distributed throughout the rubber matrix. This uniform distribution improves the efficiency of acid neutralization and promotes better cross-linking [56, 57].', '@', '@', 'Scheme 2 illustrates the proposed mechanism for the vulcanization of chlorosulfonated polyethylene rubber. In the step 1 mechanism, for sulfur vulcanization, CSM rubber must undergo thermal degradation to release its sulfonyl chloride group, enabling the formation of unsaturated bonds in the main chain that facilitate cross-linking [58]. TMTD and MBTS accelerators were employed in the PMTS1 curing process in the presence of MgO and pentaerythritol. Based on credible sources, this schematic aims to elucidate the function of each component involved in rubber cross-linking and curing. The \xadMg2+ atom’s inability to form a strong complex with the accelerator and its ionic interactions with the polymer chains are the fundamental differences in the mechanism of this process in the presence of MgO. It is posited that the presence of MgO as a curing activator result in the formation of a greater quantity of magnesium dithiocarbamate. Nevertheless, the final crosslinks are not catalyzed by the resulting compound. In the presence of MgO, it may produce dithiocarbamate more rapidly due to the higher basicity of MgO. However, it lacks catalytic activity with sulfur to form an active sulfurating complex, resulting in an increase in the vulcanization rate. From this viewpoint, MgO acts as an alkali that stabilizes the dithiocarbamate and protects it from thermal decomposition [25, 59]. Consequently, the radical sulfurization of rubber chains is the responsibility of TMTD and MBTS. The primary catalytic species in the reaction is TMTD, which is attributed to its higher activity and concentration. Additionally, MBTS has a synergistic effect on TMTD’s activity [25, 27, 60, 61]. Due to its Lewis basicity, MgO can enhance hydrogen transfer and facilitate the progression of the rubber sulfurization process (Scheme 3).', '@', '@', '@', '@', 'In step 2 of the mechanism, heat induces the opening of the sulfur ring, and during accelerated vulcanization with TMTD, a TMTD-derived sulfurating agent bonds to the CSM rubber via an allylic moiety, resulting in crosslink formation. This mechanism produces dithiocarbamate from the cleavage of the Sx-S bond of TMTD, along with water as by-products. The proposed mechanism omits the reaction of MBTS and MBT with sulfur due to the elevated concentration of the TMTD accelerator in the formula. However, the same reaction that occurs for sulfur and TMTD also occurs for MBTS and MBT [62, 63]. In step 3 of the mechanism, the crosslink precursor is generated, and subsequently, in step 4 of the mechanism, a crosslink is established between the two CSM rubber chains.', 'Step 5 clearly illustrates how, in the absence of MgO in the curing reaction, the existing dithiocarbamate is thermally degraded into dimethylamine and carbon disulfide. The curing reaction then slows down and eventually stops because dithiocarbamate recovery is impossible. Step 6 indicates that magnesium dithiocarbamate is formed more quickly because of the reaction of dithiocarbamate with MgO in the presence of pentaerythritol. The conversion of MgO into the \xadMg2+ dithiocarbamate complex also occurs using the hydrochloric acid that was released in step 1. The findings indicate that magnesium oxide, when combined with pentaerythritol, significantly improves sulfur vulcanization activity, whereas MgO alone exhibits restricted efficacy in activating the curing system. In accordance with the mechanism of step 7, magnesium dithiocarbamate can exhibit synergism with MBTS, resulting in an increased cross-linking network relative to systems utilizing individual accelerators. This complex subsequently interacts with MBTS to produce the MBT accelerator and regenerate TMTD, thereby inhibiting the thermal degradation of dithiocarbamate [25].', 'Conclusion', 'The sample containing pentaerythritol, MBTS, TMTD, and sulfur exhibits the maximum rate of vulcanization, as indicated by the results of rheometric tests and viscoelastic properties. Also, the loss factor is the lowest in this sample and the compound without pentaerythritol. Due to the marching modulus phenomenon, a very long optimal curing time, and a low curing rate index, the sample lacking pentaerythritol is not a suitable compound when compared to the formulation containing all of the ingredients. This results from the lack of pentaerythritol in the presence of magnesium oxide within the formulation during the curing reaction. Nevertheless, this compound exhibits a slightly more extensive cross-linking network. Based on the results of the DSC test, the compound that contains all of the ingredients has the highest curing enthalpy, while the sample that lacks pentaerythritol has the lowest. The elevated curing enthalpy of this compound results from efficient curing and enhanced cross-linking network formation. Conversely, the formulation devoid of pentaerythritol exhibits a lower curing enthalpy due to the slow and insufficient curing process, which is a consequence of the absence of pentaerythritol during the curing process. Dehydrochlorination has a maximum temperature of 259 °C in the MBTS-free formulation. 256 °C is the temperature of the compound that contains all ingredients. It is evident that the type of linkages tends toward mono- and disulfide as the crosslink network is formed. The reason for this is the increased dissociation energy, which can also elevate the temperature of rubber degradation and increase the temperature of HCl formation.', 'According to FTIR studies, all of the compounds possess sulfide bonds. Nevertheless, the sulfur-free compound also possesses an ionic bond that is the result of metal oxide cross-linking. The extent of sulfide cross-linking is attributable to the utilization of TMTD, which functions as both an accelerator and a sulfur donor.', 'In the absence of sulfur in the CSM rubber curing system and the use of a sulfur donor, it is possible for two sulfide and metal oxide curing mechanisms to exist simultaneously, as indicated by the results. Despite the fact that this reaction is highly unstable due to ionic bonds, it occurs at an extremely fast and low cross-linking rate. However, the dithiocarbamate will undergo thermal degradation and TMTD recovery will be impossible if pentaerythritol is not used in conjunction with MgO. The proposed mechanism and the function of each material in the formulations are established by the results of rheometric, DSC, and FTIR analysis, which exhibit a significant degree of concordance.', '@', '@', '@', '@', 'Author contribution Mohammad Reza Pourhossaini conceptualized and designed the study.
