2025-05-20 14:43:56 - WARNING: ['Investigation of poly(benzodifurandione) for bioelectronics: high conductivity, electrical stability, and biocompatibility', 'Seungju Kang1 · Eun Chae Kim2 · Hyung Woo Kim3 · Boseok Kang1,4,5', 'Received: 9 January 2025 / Revised: 26 January 2025 / Accepted: 31 January 2025 / Published online: 7 March 2025  © The Author(s) 2025', 'Abstract', 'Poly(benzodifurandione) (PBFDO), a recently developed n-type conductive polymer, shows promise as an alternative mate-rial for bioelectronics, particularly in neural probes. This study systematically evaluates the electrical, mechanical, and biocompatibility properties of PBFDO and compares its performance with the widely used material for bioelectronics; poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene) polystyrene sulfonate (PEDOT:PSS). The intrinsic doping mechanism of PBFDO provides high electrical conductivity (up to 2000 S/cm) without requiring external dopants, enhancing its environmental stability and simplifying fabrication. Surface characterizations revealed uniform coatings and hydrophilic properties suitable for bioelectronics. Notably, PBFDO demonstrated exceptional electrical stability in phosphate-buffered saline (PBS), retaining 97% of its initial conductivity after three days. Biocompatibility assays using NIH-3T3 fibroblast cells showed no cytotoxic effects, with cell proliferation rates comparable to bare glass and crosslinked PEDOT:PSS. These findings establish PBFDO as a robust and biocompatible material for next-generation bioelectronic devices, including neural probes, biosensors, and implantable electrodes.', 'Graphical abstract', 'We highlight PBFDO as a promising biocompatible electrode material for neural probes. PBFDO demonstrates intrinsically high conductivity, exceptional stability in aqueous environments, and excellent biocompatibility, all without the need for modification or post-treatment, outperforming PEDOT:PSS. These properties make PBFDO an ideal candidate for use in neural probes, offering superior material performance.', '@', 'Keywords PBFDO · Electrical conductivity · Electrical stability · Biocompatibility · Bioelectronics', '1 Introduction', 'Neural probes play an essential role in neuroscience and biomedical engineering, offering efficient tools for recording and stimulating neural activity [1–5]. These devices provide critical insights into neural circuitry, enabling advancements in treatments for neurological disorders and the development of brain-machine inter-faces. The functionality and durability of neural probes are highly dependent on the properties of the electrode materials. Traditional materials, including metals and doped semiconductors, often encounter limitations such as high impedance, mechanical stiffness mismatch, and poor biocompatibility, which are critical for bioapplica-tions [6, 7]. Furthermore, these materials can provoke immune responses, potentially leading to inflammation, tissue damage, and impaired device performance [6, 8]. These challenges necessitate the development of alterna-tive materials that combine high electrical conductivity, adjustable mechanical stiffness to match biological tissues, and excellent biocompatibility.', 'Organic electrode materials, with their tunable proper-ties enabled by modifications to their molecular structure, are attractive candidates for addressing the limitations of traditional electrode materials [9–13]. Among these, conductive polymers have garnered significant attention due to high electrical conductivity, tunable mechanical characteristics, and potential biocompatibility [14, 15]. Poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene) polystyrene sulfonate (PEDOT:PSS) is widely recognized as a leading con-ductive polymer [16]. However, PEDOT:PSS has cer-tain drawbacks for use in bioengineering applications in terms of a requirement to satisfy biocompatibility, high electrical conductivity, and stability under wet condition, at once [17]. The acidity of PSS chain, which is a well-known factor causing degradation in electronic devices, can damage on living organisms from a long-term per-spective [18]. Moreover, additives or post-treatments to enhance electrical conductivity by removing exces-sive PSS imposes significant adverse effect on bio-compatibility of PEDOT:PSS [19]. On the other hand, poly(benzodifurandione) (PBFDO) has recently emerged as a conductive polymer with notable advantages [20]. Unlike PEDOT:PSS, PBFDO does not require additives or post-treatments to enhance its electrical conductivity, avoiding risk factors (Fig. 1). In addition, according to the previous report [20], PBFDO shows superior chemical sta-bilities for various acids, base, and organic solvents, with enhanced environmental tolerance. Despite these promis-ing properties, the biocompatibility of PBFDO remains underexplored, which limits its immediate application in bioelectronics. This study evaluates the potential of PBFDO as a biocompatible electrode by systematically assessing its conductivity, electrical stability, and cell viability.', '2 \x07Experimental', '2.1 \x07Materials', '3,7-Dihydrobenzo[1,2-b:4,5-b′]difuran-2,6-dione was pur-chased from Alfa Chemistry. Duroquinone was purchased from Tokyo Chemical Industry (TCI) Co. Ltd. Anhy-drous DMSO and acetic acid (≥ 99%) was purchased from Sigma–Aldrich. PH1000 (PEDOT:PSS) was purchased from Heraeus. Dialysis tubing (MWCO: 10 kDa) was purchased from Thermo Fisher. 3-Aminopropyltriethoxysilane was pur-chased from Gelest.', '2.2 \x07PBFDO synthesis', 'At room temperature, 3,7-dihydrobenzo[1,2-b:4,5-b′]difuran-2,6-dione (400 mg, 2.1 mmol, 1 eq) and duroqui-none (690 mg, 4.2 mmol, 2 eq) were added to a 100 mL Schlenk tube, evacuated under vacuum for 60 min, and purged with nitrogen. Anhydrous DMSO (25 mL, 99.9%) was then introduced into the reaction vessel. The mixture was heated gradually to 100 °C with vigorous stirring and allowed to react for 90 min. Following the reaction, 50 mL of DMSO was added to dilute the solution, which was sub-sequently transferred into dialysis tubing (MWCO: 10 kDa). Dialysis was performed in DMSO, with solvent changes every two days over a total duration of 9 days. The puri-fied solution was concentrated using a rotary evaporator, and the concentration was determined by drying 1 mL of the solution and weighing the resulting solid. A final volume of 58 mL of PBFDO solution at 5 mg/mL was obtained, cor-responding to a yield of 72%.', '@', '@', '2.3 \x07Thin film fabrication', 'Glass substrates were cleaned by sonication in the sequence of acetone and isopropyl alcohol. The cleaned substrates were then under UVO treatment for 30 min. To fabricate self-assembled monolayer of 3-aminopropyltriethoxysilane (APTES), those substrates were submerged into the solution of 0.1 mL APTES, 1 μL acetic acid, and 10 mL de-ionized water. After 60 min, the substrates were cleaned with de-ionized water. PBFDO thin films were then fabricated on those APTES-treated substrates by off-center spin-coating at 1500 rpm, followed by drying in vacuum chamber for two hours. For the PEDOT:PSS, 15 v/v% DMSO was mixed with the aqueous dispersion and stirred for six hours at room temperature, followed by on-center spin-coating at 1500 rpm and annealing at 140°C for 30 min.', '2.4 \x07Surface characterization', 'Contact angle measurements were performed using a contact angle analyzer (SmartDrop, Femtobiomed). Conductivity measurements through the four-point probe method were performed by Keithley 2400 SourceMeter in ambient con-dition. Surface topographic images of the PBFDO thin film were obtained by non-contact mode AFM imaging with an NX-10 from Park Systems, using the NCHR tip as the AFM probe. XPS measurements were performed using NEXSA G2 instrument (Thermo Fisher Scientific).', '2.5 \x07Cell culture', 'NIH-3T3 cells were purchased from the Korean Cell Line Bank (KCLB, 21658). For proliferation, NIH-3T3 cells were cultured in Dulbecco’s Modified Essential Medium (DMEM) medium containing with 10% Fetal Bovine Serum (FBS, Gibco, USA), and 1% Penicillin–Streptomycin (Pen Strep, Gibco, USA) at 37℃ and 5% (v/v) \xadCO2. The growth medium was exchanged every 2 days.', '2.6 \x07Biocompatibility analysis', 'The cytotoxicity of the NIH 3T3 cells were analyzed through cell viability assays on bare glass substrate, PBFDO and cross-linked PEDOT:PSS. All samples were prepared in the 24 well cell culture plate. These samples were sterilized through a 70% ethanol for 10 min, followed by washing with DI water. NIH-3T3 cells were then seeded with growth medium at a density of 1 × \xad104 cells/well. The cells were incubated at 37°C and 5% \xadCO2 environment, with changing the fresh medium every 2 days. At 1, 3 and 7 days after cell seeding, cell viability was analyzed through Cell Counting Kit-8 (CCK-8, CK 04-20; Dojindo Molecular Technologies, Japan), following the manufacturer’s instruction. Briefly, adding 1 mL of 10% CCK-8 solution to each well and the samples were incubated at 37°C with 5% CO₂ for 3 h. Opti-cal density (OD) values were quantitatively measured at 450 nm using a microplate reader (BioTek 800 TS, Agilent, USA).', '3 \x07Results and discussion', '3.1 \x07Synthesis of PBFDO', 'The unique mechanism of PBFDO synthesis is in-situ dop-ing polymerization. Unlike conventional conjugated poly-mers that require an additional doping step to achieve high electrical conductivity, PBFDO exhibits an intrinsically high conductivity of up to 2000 S/cm, even without external dop-ing [20]. This remarkable conductivity arises from the dual role of the oxidant, which not only facilitates the formation of radicals in the monomer to enable polymerization but also induces protonation of the ester group in PBFDO, result-ing in n-type doping (Scheme 1). We synthesized PBFDO according to previous reports [20, 21], and the characteriza-tion was followed. UV–vis absorption spectra of the synthe-sized PBFDO revealed broad (bi)polaron absorption bands, indicative of the highly doped state (Fig. 2a). O 1s X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis confirmed the high doping level, with the protonated ester peak observed at approximately 534.7 eV (Fig. 2b). The area ratio of the protonated ester groups accounted for 45% of the total ester groups, indicating that 0.9 of the two ester groups per repeat-ing unit were protonated, corresponding to a doping effi-ciency of 0.9 electrons per repeating unit.', 'The four-point-probe measurement confirmed that thethin film fabricated with the synthesized PBFDO showedelectrical conductivity of up to 2022 S/cm (Fig. 2c), consist-ent with the previous report [20].', 'The intrinsically high conductivity of PBFDO offers sev-eral advantages compared to PEDOT:PSS, a widely usedconjugated polymer in bioengineering applications. By eliminating the need for additional processing steps, PBFDO simplifies fabrication and reduces potential sources of vari-ability. Unlike PEDOT:PSS, which often relies additives to achieve biocompatibility, high conductivity, and stability in aqueous solution [15], PBFDO stands itself without the need for additional modifications. Therefore, the intrinsically high conductivity not only simplifies the manufacturing process but also minimizes the risk of introducing external factors that could compromise device reliability, particularly in long-term bioengineering applications.', '@', '@', '@', '@', '3.2 \x07Surface characteristics', 'The atomic force microscopy (AFM) images of both PEDOT:PSS and PBFDO thin films reveal well-dispersed and uniformly coated surfaces, indicating successful film formation (Fig. 3a). The absence of significant surface irregularities or aggregation suggests that both materials achieve homogeneous coatings, which is critical for main-taining consistent electrical properties and ensuring reliable performance in bioelectronic applications. This uniformcoating further supports their suitability for use in neuralinterfaces, where surface consistency is essential for mini-mizing impedance variability and enhancing signal fidelity.', 'The hydrophilicity of PEDOT:PSS and PBFDO thin films were evaluated using water contact angle (WCA) measure-ments (Fig. 3b). To ensure the stability of the films in aque-ous environments, substrates were pre-treated with 3-ami-nopropyltriethoxysilane (APTES) to form a self-assembled monolayer, enhancing film adhesion. Both PBFDO and PEDOT:PSS films displayed WCAs below 90°, indicative of hydrophilic surfaces. The WCA of the PBFDO thin film (53.0°) was lower than the previously reported value for PBFDO films (75.4°). This discrepancy is attributed to the smoother surface morphology of our film, which has a root-mean-square roughness (Rq) of 1.145 nm. Moreover, the WCA can be significantly affected by the surface energy of the APTES monolayer fabricated on the substrate beneath the thin film (thickness < 30 nm) [22]. This hydrophilic-ity confirms the compatibility of PBFDO thin films with aqueous systems, a critical property for their application in neural interfaces.', '@', '@', '3.3 \x07Electrical stability', 'The electrical stability of PEDOT:PSS and PBFDO thin films in deionized water and phosphate-buffered saline (PBS) was examined to assess their potential as neural probe materials (Fig. 3c). The relative electrical conductiv-ity preservation was compared at three and seven days. Both PEDOT:PSS and PBFDO showed decreased conductivity in deionized water; however, PBFDO shows better stabil-ity. PEDOT:PSS retained over 64% and 62% of its initial conductivity after three and seven days, while PBFDO main-tained approximately 77% and 73%, respectively. Moreo-ver, PBFDO showed more retaining results in PBS. Notably, PBFDO demonstrated exceptional stability in PBS, retain-ing 97% and 92% of its initial conductivity over the same time frames, while PEDOT:PSS showed 59% and 56%, respectively.', 'The observed decrease in the conductivity of PEDOT:PSS can be primarily attributed to the degradation of the thin films through gradual PEDOT:PSS dissolution in the aque-ous media, as well as the defect occurrence at PEDOT backbone by aging, particularly over extended periods [23]. Even though the APTES was pre-treated on the substrates to enhance the adhesion of the PEDOT:PSS thin film, the water solubility and ionic nature of PSS make it susceptible to dis-solution of PEDOT in those aqueous media. This leachingphenomenon disrupts structural integrity and elevates sheetresistance, leading to a decline in electrical conductivity.', 'In contrast, PBFDO exhibited better stability, especially in PBS. This exceptional stability in aqueous media can be explained by its unique molecular structure and intrinsically high chemical resistance. In contrast to PEDOT:PSS, which is composed of two components, PBFDO functions indepen-dently without the need for stabilizers or secondary-bonded charge balancers, and therefore exhibits no dissolution in aqueous media. Moreover, the protonation of ester groups in PBFDO with the low Fermi level reported as − 4.67 eV creates a stable n-type doping state itself, which is less sus-ceptible to hydrolytic or oxidative degradation under physi-ological conditions [20]. The ionic composition of PBS may also stabilize the doped state of PBFDO by reducing poten-tial ion exchange processes, further preserving its conduc-tivity. These findings highlight the advantages of PBFDO over PEDOT:PSS, emphasizing its potential as a reliable material for neural probes that require long-term operation in challenging biological environments.', '3.4 \x07Biocompatibility', 'The CCK-8 assay was conducted to evaluate the biocompat-ibility of PBFDO by assessing its effects on cellular behavior over 1, 3, and 7 days (Fig. 3d). NIH-3T3 fibroblast cells were cultured on bare glass (control), PBFDO, and crosslinked PEDOT:PSS surfaces. Bare glass, a well-known biocom-patible material, served as the control group to benchmark the cytotoxic effects of PBFDO surfaces, while crosslinked PEDOT:PSS was included as a reference. In detail, (3-gly-cidyloxypropyl)trimethoxysilane (GOPS) was introduced as a crosslinking agent for PEDOT:PSS; GOPS prevents PEDOT:PSS to be dissolved in aqueous media by crosslink-ing, and the crosslinked PEDOT:PSS was previously reported as biocompatible, which makes it desirable to use as the reference [24, 25]. At all measured time points (1, 3, and 7 days), there were no statistically significant differences in absorbance values among the samples. This consistency suggests that both PBFDO and crosslinked PEDOT:PSS sur-faces are non-cytotoxic and support cell proliferation com-parable to the bare glass control. The absence of cytotoxic effects highlights the suitability of PBFDO as a material for bioelectronics, particularly in applications requiring long-term interaction with biological tissues.', 'To use as biocompatible electrodes, PEDOT:PSS must satisfy complex requirements: stability in aqueous media,sufficiently high conductivity, and biocompatibility. How-ever, PBFDO avoids these challenging hurdles by itself.Without any modification and additional process, PBFDOshows notable biocompatibility with high stability in aque-ous media and high electrical conductivity, confirming thatPBFDO is a promising material for neural probe.', '4 \x07Conclusions', 'This study highlights the potential of PBFDO as a next-generation material for bioelectronics, particularly in neu-ral probe applications. The intrinsic doping mechanism of PBFDO enables high electrical conductivity and enhanced environmental stability without requiring external dopants or additives, addressing key limitations of traditional con-jugated polymers like PEDOT:PSS. Surface characteriza-tion confirmed uniform coating and hydrophilic properties essential for effective bioelectronic interfaces. In aque-ous environments, PBFDO exhibited superior electrical stability, particularly in PBS, where it retained over 97% of its initial conductivity, underscoring its suitability for long-term use in physiological conditions. Biocompat-ibility evaluations using NIH-3T3 fibroblast cells dem-onstrated that PBFDO supports cell proliferation without cytotoxic effects, performing comparably to bare glass and crosslinked PEDOT:PSS. These results confirm that PBFDO meets the critical requirements for neural probe materials, including high conductivity, environmental stability, and biocompatibility. In conclusion, this work suggests PBFDO as a versatile and reliable material forits integration into advanced biomedical devices, includ-ing neural interfaces, biosensors, and implantable systems. 2025-05-20 14:44:22 - WARNING: ['Effect of polymer crystallinity on the spatial distribution of dopant in sequentially solution‑doped conjugated polymer thin films', 'Yongkyeong Lee1 · Seung Hyun Kim2 · Daegun Kim1 · Hansol Lee1', 'Received: 3 February 2025 / Revised: 23 April 2025 / Accepted: 28 April 2025  © The Author(s), under exclusive licence to The Polymer Society of Korea 2025', 'Abstract', 'Sequential solution doping is one of the most frequently used methods for doping conjugated polymer films. Since the spatial distribution of dopants in doped polymer films significantly influences their electrical properties, elucidating how various material parameters affect the dopant distribution in sequentially solution-doped polymer films is of great importance. This study investigates the effect of crystallinity in conjugated polymer thin films on the spatial distribution of dopants along the out-of-plane direction. The results show that higher crystallinity in polymer films leads to a more non-uniform dopant distribution, with a greater composition of dopants at the film surface compared to the bulk. This phenomenon is attributed to the crystallinity-dependent swellability of polymer films, where films with higher crystallinity exhibit lower swellability, resulting in less efficient dopant diffusion from the surface to the bulk compared to films with lower crystallinity.', 'Graphic abstract', 'This study explores how crystallinity in conjugated polymer thin films affects dopant distribution during sequential solution doping, revealing that higher crystallinity results in a more nonuniform distribution, with greater dopant compositions at the surface than in the bulk. This is due to lower swellability, which limits effective dopant diffusion from the surface to the bulk.', '@', 'Keywords Chemical doping · Conjugated polymers · Molecular dopants · Crystallinity · Sequential solution doping', '1 Introduction', 'Conjugated polymers have garnered significant attention in the field of organic electronics due to their unique optoelectronic properties [1–10]. One promising approach to enhance the performance of conjugated polymers is through doping, particularly in the field of polymer electrodes and organic thermoelectrics [3, 7, 11–16]. Doping involves introducing additional molecules into a polymer film to control the charge carrier concentration via charge transfer between them, resulting in changes to the polymer’s optical and electrical properties, such as electrical conductivity, Fermi level, and light absorption coefficients in visible and near-infrared ranges [17–19]. The development of efficient dopants and doping methods, along with a concrete understanding of the underlying principles, is essential for improving the performance of doped conjugated polymers in various applications.', 'Two commonly employed solution-based doping techniques used in the doping of conjugated polymers are mixed-solution doping and sequential solution doping. Mixed-solution doping of a conjugated polymer involves the blending of the polymer and dopant in one solution, followed by the deposition of the blend solution to obtain a doped polymer film [3]. Although this method is advantageous for the precise control of the ratio between the polymer and dopant, it often suffers from severe aggregation of charged polymers within the solution and poor quality of the resulting polymer films [20]. On the other hand, sequential solution doping of a conjugated polymer involves exposing a precast undoped polymer film to a dopant solution for an extended time, which then leads to the diffusion of dopant molecules into the polymer film [21]. This method allows for effective doping of polymer thin films without significantly damaging their microstructure, making it widely used for doping polymer thin films with optimized microstructures [18, 22].', 'Sequential solution doping, despite its advantages, presents some challenges. Since this doping method relies on the diffusion of dopant molecules within the already solidified polymer films, it may take a significant amount of time for the dopant molecules to penetrate deeply into the film [23, 24]. This means that for relatively thick polymer films, the dopant may not diffuse effectively throughout the entire film, resulting in considerable nonuniformity in dopant content and doping levels between the surface region and the bulk of the film. Non-uniform doping in thick polymer films can pose a serious drawback in the application of doped conjugated polymers. For example, in typical Π-structured thermoelectric devices based on conjugated polymers, a thick film is inevitably required to maximize the temperature difference across the device [25, 26]; however, the non-uniform doping in this thick film can lead to a significant deterioration of the thermoelectric performance of the device. In this context, understanding the distribution of dopant molecules within sequentially solution-doped polymer films and the factors influencing this distribution is crucial.', 'this distribution is crucial.Although many studies have revealed the spatial distribution of dopants within sequentially doped conjugated polymer films, they have primarily focused on where the dopants prefer to reside within the crystalline and amorphous regions of polymer films, or on how the inclusion of dopants affects the polymer’s crystalline structure [14–16, 27–30]. In contrast, the distribution of dopants along the thickness direction (i.e., along the out-of-plane direction) has received relatively little attention. A recent study demonstrated that doping of a conjugated polymer film using the immersion doping method leads to a significantly non-uniform dopant distribution along the thickness direction due to the limited diffusivity of dopants within the film [31]. While the study thoroughly examined the dopant diffusion process within a conjugated polymer film, it did not provide insights into how the film’s microstructure influences dopant diffusion behavior and spatial distribution. In this work, we investigate the effect of polymer crystallinity on the dopant distribution along the thickness direction of sequentially solution-doped conjugated polymer films. Using regioregular poly(3-hexylthiophene) (P3HT) as a model conjugated polymer, we characterized the dopant distribution by varying the crystallinity of the polymer film via thermal annealing. Under the same conditions of sequential solution doping, it was found that while the overall dopant content within two polymer films with different degrees of crystallinity was almost similar, the distribution of dopants along the depth direction was different. The film with higher crystallinity exhibited greater nonuniformity in dopant distribution, with the ratio of dopant content between the film surface and bulk being higher in the film with greater crystallinity compared to the film with lower crystallinity. These differences are attributed to different swellabilities of the polymer films due to variations in their crystallinity, which affect the diffusion of dopant molecules within the films during the doping process.', '2 \x07Experimental', '2.1 \x07Materials', 'P3HT with regioregularity of ≥ 90% was purchased from Rieke Metals Inc. (USA). 2,3,5,6-Tetrafluoro-7,7,8,8-tetracyanoquinodimethane (F4TCNQ, > 98%) was purchased from Tokyo Chemical Industry Co., Ltd. (Japan). Acetonitrile (ACN, ≥ 99.5%) and chloroform (CF, anhydrous, ≥ 99%) were purchased from Sigma-Aldrich Inc.(Germany). The materials were used without any furtherpurification.', '2.2 \x07Preparation of polymer thin films and their sequential solution doping', 'Either soda-lime glass or a Si wafer with a native oxide was used as the substrates for sample preparation. The substrates were cleaned sequentially with detergent (Extran MA 02, Merck Millipore), deionized water, acetone, and isopropyl alcohol. The substrates were treated with UV-ozone for 20 min before applying polymer solution on them. The polymer solution (5 mg \xadmL−1) was prepared by dissolving P3HT in CF at 55 °C. Then, the P3HT solution was spin-coated onto the substrates at 2000 rpm for 60 s. The resulting polymer films had a thickness of ~ 47 nm. For thermal annealing, P3HT films were placed on hot plates with a surface temperature of 200 °C for 10 min. For sequential solution doping, the dopant solution was prepared by dissolving F4TCNQ in ACN at room temperature. The concentration of the F4TCNQ dopant solution varied from 0.15 to 2.0 wt%. Doping was performed by dropping the dopant solution onto the polymer film to cover the entire surface and maintaining it for a minute to allow diffusion of dopant molecules into the polymer film. Then, the residual dopant solution was removed by spin-coating. After the doping process, doped polymer films were thermally annealed for 5 min at a mild temperature of 85 °C, which is slightly above the boiling temperature of ACN, to completely dry the doped polymer films. The entire processes of solution preparation, spin-coating, thermal annealing, and sequential solution doping were performed in a nitrogen-filled glove box.', '2.3 \x07Measurement of swelling ratio of polymer films', 'The swelling ratio of polymer films was calculated from the thicknesses of the polymer films before and after swelling in ACN. To swell the polymer films, pure ACN was poured into a glass petri dish, and the polymer films coated on Si substrates were immersed in the ACN for a minute. After being lifted from the ACN, the surface of polymer films was dried quickly by blowing with nitrogen gas to eliminate any residual solvent droplets. Then, the thicknesses of the polymer films were measured using ellipsometry (M-2000 ellipsometer, J.A. Woollam), using the Cauchy model to fit the recorded spectra in the 860–1000 nm wavelength range. Grazing-incidence X-ray diffraction (GIXD) measurementswere performed at the 3C and 9A beamlines of Pohang', '2.4 \x07Other characterizations Accelerator Laboratory (PAL), Korea. The photon energy was 9.30 keV (λ = 1.33 Å). The sample-to-detector distance was 214 mm. The incidence angle of X-ray beam was set around the critical angle of the polymer film. To compare the surface structure and the bulk structure of the polymer films, the GIXD data were recorded by varying the incidence angle by 0.5° above and below the critical angle. Ultraviolet–visible–near-infrared (UV–Vis–NIR) absorption spectra were obtained using a V-770 spectrometer (Jasco). Fourier-transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra were obtained using a VERTEX 70v FT-IR spectrometer (Bruker).', '3 \x07Results and discussion', '3.1 \x07Conjugated polymer thin films with different crystallinities', 'In this study, regioregular P3HT was chosen as a model conjugated polymer (Fig. 1a). P3HT thin films with different crystallinities were prepared to investigate the effect of polymer crystallinity on the dopant diffusion into polymer thin films during solution sequential doping. F4TCNQ, one of the most widely used molecular dopants for p-doping of conjugated polymers, was used in this study to dope the P3HT films via the sequential solution doping method. ACN was selected as the solvent for the dopant solution. ACN is a poor solvent for P3HT but dissolves F4TCNQ well, so it is widely used for sequential solution doping of P3HT films [21, 30, 32].', 'Thermal annealing of P3HT films at elevated temperatures is known to increase the crystallinity of the films [33]. To confirm the increased crystallinity of thermally annealed P3HT (P3HT-T) film compared to that of as-prepared P3HT (P3HT-A) film, two-dimensional (2D) GIXD measurements were performed (Fig. 1b and c). Figure 1b shows the 2D image of the diffraction pattern of P3HT-A and P3HT-T films, and Fig. 1c shows the corresponding one-dimensional diffraction intensity profiles along the in-plane and out-of-plane directions. The diffraction patterns showed (h00) peaks and (010) peaks along the in-plane and out-of-plane directions, indicating a bimodal orientation distribution (i.e., both edge-on and face-on orientations coexist) of P3HT crystallites. Compared to the P3HT-A film, the P3HT-T film showed much stronger (h00) peak intensities along the out-of-plane direction and (010) peak intensity along the in-plane direction. This result indicates that the P3HT-T film has greater crystallinity than the P3HT-A film, and that P3HT-T film has a greater portion of edge-on oriented crystallites among the bimodal orientation distribution compared to the P3HT-A film.', '@', '@', '3.2 \x07Spectroscopic characterizations on doped polymer films', 'To compare the doping behavior of P3HT-A and P3HT-T films during the sequential solution doping process, the polymer films were doped with the F4TCNQ dopant solution dissolved in ACN. The details of the sequential solution doping process can be found in the Experimental section.', 'FT-IR spectra of undoped and doped P3HT-A and P3HT-T films were measured to confirm the presence of dopant molecules within the doped polymer films (Fig. 2a). In both P3HT-A and P3HT-T, regardless of whether they were doped or not, the vibronic peaks at 2800–3000 \xadcm−1, which corresponded to C–H stretching of alkanes in their hexyl side chains [34], were commonly observed. The presence of F4TCNQ in the doped polymer films was evident from the appearance of vibronic peaks at 2170 and 2189 \xadcm−1, which corresponded to C≡N stretching in F4TCNQ anions [27]. For reference, the C≡N-stretching peak of a neutral F4TCNQ molecule is known to appear at ~ 2230 \xadcm−1 [27]. When the absorption spectra of doped P3HT-A and doped P3HT-T films were normalized by the absorption maximum of the C–H-stretching peaks of each spectrum, the normalized absorption intensities of the C≡N-stretching peaks were nearly the same (Fig. 2b), suggesting nearly the same amount of F4TCNQ anions in the two films.', '@', '@', 'UV–Vis–NIR absorption spectra were obtained to assess the occurrence and extent of doping in P3HT-A and P3HT-T before and after sequential solution doping. The left and right panels of Fig. 2c represent the absorption spectra of P3HT-A and P3HT-T films, respectively, sequentially solution-doped with the dopant solutions at various dopant concentrations. Both P3HT-A and P3HT-T films showed similar changes in spectral shapes upon doping. In the undoped state, they showed an absorption band in the visible-wavelength range at around 300–650 nm, with a maximum absorption at 550 nm. The higher crystallinity of the P3HT-T film compared to that of the P3HT-A film could be confirmed again by the more pronounced absorption shoulder at ~ 600 nm in the P3HT-T film [33]. Upon doping the polymer films with F4TCNQ, a decrease in the initial absorption at around 500 nm and the emergence of a broad absorption peak between 1000 and 2000 nm were observed, due to the formation of positive polarons in P3HT [27, 35]. Additionally, distinct peaks at 413, 770, and 870 nm appeared in the spectra of doped polymer films, which are well known to be associated with the F4TCNQ anions formed by the electron transfer from P3HT to F4TCNQ upon doping [27]. The decrease in the initial absorption intensity and the increase of newly formed absorption peaks due to doping became more pronounced as the dopant concentration in solution increased, indicating a higher doping level of P3HT for the film doped with the dopant solution of greater concentration. When comparing the spectra of P3HT-A and P3HT-T films doped at the same dopant concentration, the intensities of P3HT positive polarons and F4TCNQ anions were slightly greater in P3HT-T than in P3HT-A; this was observed for all dopant concentrations tested. The greater polymer positive polaron and dopant anion intensities in P3HT-T than in P3HT-A, despite nearly the same dopant contents within them, suggested that the ionization of the dopant was slightly more efficient in the P3HT-T film than in the P3HT-A film, likely due to its greater degree of crystallinity and charge delocalization [36].', '3.3 \x07Effect of polymer crystallinity on the dopant distribution within polymer films', 'The effect of the polymer film’s crystallinity on the spatialdistribution of dopant molecules along the out-of-plane direction was investigated based on the structural analysis of the doped polymer films using 2D GIXD (Fig. 3). 2D GIXD measurements were performed at different X-ray incidence angles to compare the surface structure and the bulk structure of the doped P3HT films. At low incidence angles below the critical angle of the polymer film, the incident beam interacts with the polymer film at only a short distance below the film surface. In contrast, at high incidence angles above the critical angle of the polymer film, the incident beam penetrates the polymer film and interacts primarily with the bulk of the film. Therefore, comparing GIXD data obtained slightly below and above the critical angle is an effective way to assess the surface and bulk structures of the polymer films [37, 38].', 'Figure 3a shows the 2D images of GIXD patterns for doped P3HT-A and doped P3HT-T films obtained at low and high incidence angles. Figure 3b presents the one-dimensional intensity profiles along the out-of-plane direction derived from the 2D images in Fig. 3a. The profiles of undoped films are also shown in the plot for comparison. The (h00) and (010) diffraction peaks of P3HT crystals were observed in the doped films, similar to those in the undoped films. However, the peak positions shifted significantly upon doping; the (h00) peaks shifted toward smaller q values, while the (010) peak shifted toward larger q values. This trend is consistent with the previous reports [21, 28, 32], and such changes in crystal structure indicate that F4TCNQ molecules are incorporated within P3HT crystallites, especially in the lamellar regions between hexyl side chains of P3HT [28, 29]. In addition to the diffraction peaks of P3HT, the doped P3HT films also showed a new diffraction peak at q =  ~ 0.84 Å−1, which is assigned to the diffraction pattern that arises from F4TCNQ [27, 39].', 'Doped P3HT-A and P3HT-T films showed distinct X-ray incident angle dependence in their peak positions. While the doped P3HT-A film exhibited nearly the same (100) and (010) peak positions regardless of the incidence angle, the doped P3HT-T film displayed different (100) and (010) peak positions depending on the incidence angle. In the case of the doped P3HT-A, the (010) peak appeared at q = 1.691 and 1.689 Å−1 for high and low incidence angles, respectively, indicating virtually the same peak positions. The position of the (100) peak could not be quantified accurately due to its weak and broad nature. In contrast, in the case of the doped P3HT-T, the (010) peak appeared at q = 1.715 and 1.743 Å−1 for high and low incidence angles, respectively, while the (100) peak appeared at q = 0.341 and 0.333 Å−1 for high and low incidence angles, respectively. This suggests that the crystalline structure at the surface region of the doped P3HT-T film is quite different from that of the bulk region, with the surface region exhibiting more increased lamellar spacing and more decreased π–π stacking distance compared to thoseof the film’s bulk. The changes in π–π stacking distanceand lamellar spacing are presented in Fig. 3c.', '@', '@', '@', 'Considering that the π–π stacking distance decreases more and the lamellar spacing increases more with respect to the increasing amount of dopant molecules incorporated into the P3HT crystals [32], the observed dependence of the diffraction peak position of doped P3HT-T on the X-ray incidence angle implies that the dopant concentration near the surface of doped P3HT-T is relatively greater than that in the bulk. On the other hand, the independence of the diffraction peak position of doped P3HT-A from the X-ray incidence angle indicates that there is no significant difference in the dopant concentration between the surface and the bulk regions of doped P3HT-A film.', 'It should be noted that, as confirmed earlier by the FT-IR spectroscopy results, the amount of dopant molecules present in the two polymer films is almost similar. Therefore, the above GIXD results do not arise from the difference in the absolute content of dopants within the two P3HT films, but rather from the different degrees of dopant diffusion from polymer surface toward the bulk, for the same overall of dopant contents within the films. That is, the diffusion of the dopant into the bulk of the film was less efficient in the more crystalline P3HT-T, resulting in a greater amount of dopant near the surface compared to the bulk.', '3.4 \x07Discussion on the origin of crystallinity‑dependent dopant distribution', 'It has been reported that the degree of swelling of a polymer thin film by solvents depends on the crystallinity of the polymer film; the higher the crystallinity of a conjugated polymer thin film, the lower the swelling ratio of the film, in general [40–43]. According to previous studies on the swelling of semicrystalline polymers, polymer crystallites are hardly penetrable to solvents, and thus, swelling occurs predominantly in the amorphous phase of the polymer films [42, 43]. Therefore, the trend of decreasing swelling ratio with respect to increasing polymer crystallinity can be understood as a result of the greater content of polymer crystallites within the film with higher crystallinity than that within the film with lower crystallinity. Based on this knowledge, we speculated that the swellability of P3HT thin films would change as a result of varied crystallinity of the films via thermal annealing; that is, the P3HT-T film would exhibit a smaller swelling ratio than the P3HT-A film when they reached swelling equilibrium with ACN due to its greater crystallinity. This speculation was shown to be valid by directly measuring the thickness of P3HT-A and P3HT-T films before and after swelling (Fig. 4). Film thicknesses were measured using an ellipsometer, fitting the recorded spectral data using the Cauchy model. In the fitting process, only the wavelength region of 860–1000 nm was used to avoid the excitonic absorption peaks of the polymer films. In swelling the polymer films, pure ACN was used as the swelling agent, because it is the solvent of the F4TCNQ dopant solution used in our sequential solution doping process. The detailed procedure for swelling polymer films is provided in the Experimental section. After dipping the P3HT-A and P3HT-T polymer films in pure ACN for 5 min, the thickness of the films increased considerably compared to the initial (i.e., dried-state) thickness, indicating the uptake of ACN molecules in the polymer films. While the thicknesses of the dried P3HT-A and P3HT-T films are almost identical at ~ 47 nm, the thicknesses of the swelled films were significantly different, showing 53 (± 1.5) and 49 (± 0.2) nm for P3HT-A and P3HT-T, respectively (Fig. 4a). The corresponding percent increase in the film thickness was 11.3% for P3HT-A and 4.0% for P3HT-T (Fig. 4b). These results clearly show that the swellability of the P3HT films depends significantly on their crystallinities.', 'We expect that the crystallinity-dependent dopant distribution originates mainly from the different swellability of the polymer films. Swelling of polymer films by dopant solvent is known to be essential for the efficient diffusion of dopant molecules into polymer films during sequential solution doping process [20, 22, 43]. Previous studies have reported that the more a polymer swells due to the dopant solvent, the easier the dopant diffusion can occur during sequential solution doping [20, 22]. Therefore, it is likely that the lower degree of swelling of P3HT-T compared to P3HT-A, due to its higher crystallinity, would lead to less-effective dopant diffusion into the bulk of the polymer film, resulting in the pronounced nonuniformity of the F4TCNQ distribution along the depth direction in doped P3HT-T film compared to that of doped P3HT-A film. The effect of polymer crystallinity on the dopant distribution of sequentially solution-doped polymer film is depicted schematically in Fig. 4c.', 'One might think that the different diffusion rates of dopants in the crystalline and amorphous regions of P3HT would also affect the dopant distribution in doped P3HT-A and P3HT-T films. Previous studies investigating the correlation between dopant diffusion and the ordering of conjugated polymers have demonstrated that dopant molecules tend to diffuse more slowly in disordered regions than in ordered regions of polythiophene-based conjugated polymer thin films [23, 44]. Based on this, if the different dopant distributions in P3HT-A and P3HT-T films originated from different diffusion rates of dopants in crystalline and amorphous regions, the dopant distribution should be more uniform along the depth direction in P3HT-T than of dopant diffusion behavior into polymer films with differentcrystallinities and swellabilities. The consequent dopant distributionwithin each film is also demonstrated', '@', '@', 'in P3HT-A due to its higher crystallinity; however, our experimental observations indicate the opposite. Therefore, the observed dopant distribution cannot be explained based on different diffusion coefficients of dopants in crystalline and amorphous regions. Instead, it is more plausible that the different dopant distributions in P3HT-A and P3HT-T films are primarily attributed to their different degrees of swelling during the sequential solution doping process.', '4 \x07Conclusions', 'In summary, we investigated the effect of polymer crystallinity on the spatial distribution of dopants, especially along the direction perpendicular to the film surface, within sequentially solution-doped polymer films. The crystallinity of conjugated polymer films was controlled by thermal annealing, which resulted in greater crystallinity for the thermally annealed film compared to that of the as-prepared film. When the two films were sequentially doped with a dopant solution under the same conditions, they showed nearly the same dopant content over the entire film but had different dopant distributions along the depth direction; the film with greater crystallinity exhibited a more uneven distribution of the dopant compared to that with lower crystallinity, with a higher composition of the dopant at the surface than in the bulk. The difference in the dopant distributions was attributed to the different degree of swelling between the two films. That is, the film with higher crystallinity swelled less by the dopant solvent, which likely resulted in a relatively less efficient diffusion of the dopant from the surface into the bulk, leading to a higher concentration of dopant near the surface compared to the bulk. The findings of this study provide a deep understanding of how the structure of polymer films affects the diffusion behavior of dopants during the sequential solution doping process and are believed to offer insights into more efficient and systematic control of doping in conjugated polymer thin films.', 'Acknowledgements Y. Lee and S. H. Kim contributed equally to this work. This work was supported by the National Research Foundation of Korea (NRF) grant funded by the Korea government (MSIT) (No. RS-2022-00166297). This work was also supported by the Gachon University Research Fund of 2023 (GCU-202301060001). The authors would like to thank the Pohang Accelerator Laboratory for providing synchrotron radiation sources at the 3C and 9A beamlines used in this study.', 'Author contributions YL, SHK: conceptualization, methodology, formal analysis, data curation, writing—original draft, and writing—review and editing. DK: formal analysis. HL: supervision, funding acquisition, and writing—review and editing.', 'Funding National Research Foundation of Korea, RS-2022-00166297, Hansol Lee,Gachon University, GCU-202301060001, Hansol Lee.', 'Declarations', 'Conflict of interest The authors declare no competing interests.', 'Yongkyeong Lee1 · Seung Hyun Kim2 · Daegun Kim1 · Hansol Lee1', '* Hansol Lee  hlee22@gachon.ac.kr']

2025-05-20 14:45:03 - WARNING: ['Crystallization kinetics, spherulitic growth and morphology  development in non‑polar polymer blend with strong  dynamic asymmetry', 'Nithin Chandran1,2 · C. Sarathchandran3 · Anjaly Sivadas1 · Robert A. Shanks4 The crystallization kinetics, spherulitic growth rate and morphology development of a non-polar blend mixture [isotactic polypropylene (iPP) and natural rubber (NR)] with strong dynamic asymmetry are investigated comprehensively. Melt mixed samples of iPP and natural NR with different NR loading were subjected to ther-mal analysis using differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) and thermogravimetric (TGA) followed by crystal growth using optical microscopy (OM). Prior to melt mixing, the NR samples were masticated for 30 min so as to reduce the molecular weight. Detailed evaluation of the kinetics of crystallization, growth rate of crystals, and morphology development in a dynamically asymmetric system is challenging especially when the second component is a semi-crystalline polymer and studies on such systems have not received significant attention.', 'Received: 27 December 2024 / Revised: 14 February 2025 / Accepted: 9 March 2025  © The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer-Verlag GmbH Germany, part of Springer Nature 2025', 'Abstract', 'Introduction', 'Mixing two or more polymers is a well-established approach for designing materials with specific end use [1]. Changes in inherent characteristics and processing conditions of the component polymers are the main factors that influence the ultimate properties of polymer blends. Morphology and thermal properties of individual polymers play a major role in the precise manipulation of the properties (mechanical, optical, electrical, etc.), thereby leading to the successful development of polymer blends with a unique set of properties. The situation is far more challenging when one of the blend compo-nents is a semi-crystalline polymer due to several competing reactions, including phase separation, nucleation and crystal growth, ripening of crystal structure, etc. However, semi-crystalline polymers and their blends are quite extensively used in the automo-bile industry. Thereby, it is of industrial/academic interest to study the crystallization behavior of immiscible blend systems especially with dynamic asymmetry. This can be achieved by the addition of a high molecular weight additive to the semi-crystalline polymer matrix [2–4]. The influence of introducing low molecular weight additives [5–7] and fillers [8–10] have been extensively investigated. Even then, the effect of add-ing high molecular weight additives such as elastomers, block copolymers [11, 12] etc., to a semi-crystalline polymer matrix has not received significant attention.', 'The ease of processability, coupled with desirable characteristics/properties, makes polypropylene (iPP) an appealing thermoplastic for broad applications rang-ing from the automotive industry to biomedical industry [13] making it the best candidate for the present study. Even though the crystallization kinetics and mor-phology of iPP-based blends [14–19] and the influence of additives (especially those of low molecular weight) on the crystallization and morphology of semi-crystalline polymers have already been investigated [20, 21]. The influence of adding a high molecular weight polymer on segmental dynamics, crystallization kinetics and ther-mal stability remains unexplored and requires special attention. Rheological, mor-phological and mechanical properties of high molecular weight NR blended with PP have been extensively studied [22–24]. But an in-depth analysis of the thermal prop-erties of iPP-NR blends is not available especially, while using NR as a toughening agent. The very large difference in molecular weight between iPP and NR makes them a dynamically asymmetric system. Since the role of NR as a toughening agent for iPP was the main aim of present work, fillers were not used as this would inhibit the contribution of NR to morphology development and, in turn, mechanical proper-ties. The current manuscript ushers to provide fundamental insights into the crystal-lization kinetics, morphology development and thermal stability of a dynamic asym-metric blend with a semi-crystalline component.', 'The current research deals with subjecting the blends to different thermal his-tories to determine the glass transition temperature (Tg), melting temperature (Tm), and melting enthalpy (ΔHm) using DSC. Apart from these, isothermal crystalliza-tion experiments were carried out to determine the half-time of crystallization (t0.5) and the kinetic parameters, which include the Avrami exponent (n) and the overall rate of crystallization (K1/n). Besides, the spherulitic growth of the blend has been followed using polarizing optical microscopy (POM).', 'Experimental', 'Materials', 'iPP (Repol-H350 FG) was supplied by Reliance Industries Limited with melt flow index, (230 °C/2.16 kg) of 38 g/min and heat deflection temperature (455 kPa) of 104 °C. Natural rubber (ISNR-5) was obtained from Rubber Research Institute,India, Kottayam, with an average molecular weight of ~ 7,80,000 g \xadmol−1.', 'Blend preparation', 'iPP was dried in a vacuum oven at 80 °C for 6 h. To improve mixing efficiency, the high viscosity NR was reduced by masticating it for 5 min in a two-roll mill with a nip clearance of 3 mm and a friction ratio of 1.25:1 (20/15 rpm, back roll/front roll). Blends with different blend ratios of iPP and NR (100–0, 90–10, 80–20, 70–30 60–40 and 50–50) were melt-mixed using a Brabender Plastocorder. The processing parameters, temperature, rotation per minute and time were fixed as 180 °C, 50 rpm and 10 min respectively.', 'Characterization techniques', 'Differential scanning calorimetry (DSC)', 'Perkin-Elmer (Diamond series) DSC was used to study the crystallization and melt-ing behavior, while TA instruments DSC (Q 200) was used to study the Tg of the blends. Both DSCs were calibrated with indium standard under nitrogen atmosphere that was purged throughout the analysis to minimize thermo-oxidative degradation. Samples of weight 2–3 mg were used for each DSC experiment. The samples were exposed to different thermal histories:', '1. Isothermal crystallization:- Sample was heated up from 50 °C with a rate of 10 °C\xadmin−1 It was melted at a temperature (Ta) of 200 °C for 3 min, followed by cooling at a rate of 20 °C \xadmin−1 to crystallization temperatures (Tc) in the range of 124 to 136 °C and held until complete crystallization was recorded. The half-time of crystallization (t0.5) was determined at Tc = constant  (Refer Fig. 1).2. Melting behavior: Samples were heated from 50 to 200 °C at a rate of 10 °C \xadmin−1 and held for 3 min, followed by cooling with a rate of 20 °C \xadmin−1 to selected crystallization temperature (Tc) ranging between 124 to 136 °C and held for 5 (t0.5). The sample was then reheated to 200 °C with a rate of 10 °C \xadmin−1.3. Glass transition temperature: Sample was heated from 50 to 200 °C at a rate of 10 °C \xadmin−1 and held for 3 min, cooled to – 90 °C, held for 1 min then, reheated to 200 °C at a rate of 10 °C \xadmin−1.', 'Scanning electron microscopy (SEM)', 'Morphological studies were carried out using a Scanning Electron Microscope (SEM), JEOL (model-JSM- 6390) operating at an acceleration voltage of 15 kV in a BSE (backscattering electron). Hot press compression molded samples were cryo-genically fractured in liquid nitrogen to avoid phase deformation during the fracture process. The rubber phase was preferentially extracted using toluene as the solvent. The dried surfaces of samples were platinum coated before scanning.', 'Polarizing optical microscope (POM): The diameter of the spherulites and spherulitic growth rate of blends were studied using Leica Q Win Software, which was attached to the Nikon microscope. The samples were cast onto cover slips and heated up from 30 °C with a rate of 10 °C \xadmin−1. It was annealed at Ta = 200 °C for 3 min, followed by cooling with a rate of 20 °C \xadmin−1 to a pre-selected crystalliza-tion temperature (Tc) between 124 to 136 °C and held there until complete crystal-lization. Micrographs of iPP spherulites were captured at appropriate time intervals (30 s) during isothermal crystallization.', 'Thermogravimetry', 'TGA measurements were performed using a TA instrument (SDT Q600) at a heat-ing rate of 10 °C·min−1. 10–20 mg of samples were heated from 0 to 700 °C under \xadN2 atmosphere.', 'Results and discussion', 'Table 1 represents the results of glass transition temperature (Tg) obtained duringthe second heating cycle for iPP-NR blends (Fig. 1).', 'The Tg of iPP and NR were found to be − 7.8 and − 64.3 °C respectively. Two distinct Tg observed indicate that the amorphous fraction of iPP is immiscible with NR. Tg values were calculated using the half ∆Cp extrapolation technique.', 'Cp = HF × 60 × K훽s × m0', 'where HF is the heat flow, βs represents the heating rate, m0 denotes sample mass, and K is the calibration constant, hence', 'K =standard Cpmeasured Cp,', 'Composition-dependent variation in Tg can be observed due to the formation of a phase in phase morphology. The endothermic deviations obtained during the sec-ond heating cycle can be used to calculate the extent of cooperatively rearranging regions (CRR) (ξ) for the iPP phase using Donth’s approximation (2) [25–27], whichplays a vital role in the evaluation of the crystallinity.', '@', '@', '@', '@', '휉=kT2gΔC−1p휌(훿T)2 ,', 'where ρ the density of bulk material, ΔCp−1 is the reciprocal specific heat at con-stant pressure and δT. The result obtained is shown in Fig. 2.', 'It can be observed that as the amount of NR increases, the ξ showed a gradual decrease which is in accordance with the morphology change observed using SEM. Gradual increases in the amount of NR result in a change from droplet-matrix to co-continuous type morphology. This is in exact correlation with calculated CRR values. In the case of the blend with 50%·w/w of NR, an increase in CRR can be observed due to phase inversion. The crystallization of a semi-crystalline polymer in a blend can be affected by its counterpart. Molecular structure and molecular weight of the components, blend composition, type and degree of dispersion for dif-ferent phases in the molten stage, phase interaction at the interface of the individ-ual homopolymers and crystallization conditions are some of the major factors that affect the crystallinity in the polymer. The crystallinity (χ) [28, 29] is given by,', '휒% =ΔHmΔH◦ m × Vf× 100,', 'where ΔHm and Vf are the enthalpy of fusion and volume fraction of iPP, respec-tively. The ΔH0m is the heat of fusion of 100% pure crystalline PP which was taken asiPP is 209 J·g−1. [30] The crystallinity increases with NR loading, reaches a maxi-mum of 30% w/w of NR and thereafter decreases (refer to Fig. 3). The decrease in ξ and increase in χ indicate that small fractions of NR aid the crystallization of iPP by serving as nucleating agents, and the amorphous fraction of iPP has weak van der Waal’s interaction with NR, with the interactions sensitive to NR loading. Figure 3 shows the melting curve for iPP–NR blend system (Table 2).', 'Endotherms for the second heating cycle were used to evaluate the melting behavior. This eliminates the influence of processing history on melting behavior. The melting temperature is almost the same in all blends (~ 163 °C). However, a small broadening of the melting peak can be observed, which may be due to the melting of different-sized crystals or disordered crystals formed by the addition of NR.', '@', '@', 'The endotherm obtained for iPP-NR blends after crystallization at selected crys-tallization temperatures (Tc) was used to calculate the equilibrium melting tempera-ture (Tm°) using Hoffman–Weeks Eq. (4) [31–33],', 'Tm = Tc2훽+ T0 m[1 −12훽]', 'Here Tm is the melting temperature calculated at selected Tc and 훽= lcl∗c is the thick-ening factor that is greater than or equal to one. Plot of Tmº against blend composi-tion is shown in Fig. 4.', 'The trend for Tm° has wide implications for polymer blends. An increasing value for Tm° indicates that the crystalline fraction is compatible with the added amor-phous component. An increase in Tm° is rarely encountered. A decrease in Tm° indicates attractive interactions between the blend components. It can be observed from Fig. 4 that the Tm° for iPP–NR blend shows a decreasing trend indicating that the crystalline fraction of iPP is having an attractive interaction with NR. Figure 5 shows the crystallization of iPP–NR blend at a selected Tc of 130 °C.', 'It follows from Fig. 6 that the addition of NR influenced the crystallization ofiPP, and the rate of crystallization increased with the addition of NR. The broad-ened exotherm of neat iPP sharpens with NR loading, indicating favorable inter-action between iPP and NR. Neat iPP requires more time to crystallize than that of NR blended iPP, confirming the efficiency of NR as a nucleating agent. The isothermal crystallization studies were carried out to evaluate the crystallization kinetics for iPP–NR blends as a function of NR loading. The time taken for half the crystallinity to develop (t0.5) is recommended to remove the influence of the overall rate of crystallization on the phase boundaries of a heterogeneous system. Figure 6 shows a plot of t0.5 against Tc (124–132 °C) for iPP and iPP–NR blends.', '@', '@', 'The crystalline kinetics for polymeric materials can be best explained usingthe Avrami Eq. (5) [34–36] as,', 'X(t) = 1 −exp[−K1∕n A t]n', 'The conversion, X(t), is the normalized crystallinity given as the ratio of the crystallinity at time, t, and the final crystallinity. KA1/n and n represent the over-all rate of crystallization and the Avrami exponent respectively (refer to Fig. 7). Table 2 shows the value of K and n obtained for the PP-NR blend at different Tcs.', 'The Avrami exponent n is expected to have integral values, where a value between 1 and 2 represents 1D crystal growth, values between 2 and 3 indicate 2D crystal growth, while values between 3 and 4 represent 3D crystal growth and above 4 indicate heterogeneous nucleation. iPP–NR blends show a value of n close to 3, indicating sporadic nucleation with 2 D crystal growth. The non-inte-gral value represents the non-Fickian contribution to the crystallization process. Figure 8 represents the plot of (t0.5)−1 against (ΔTc)−1.', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', '(t0.5)−1 = A exp(−BΔTc)', 'Here A is the frequency factor, and B is the ratio of energy densities in the lamella fold surface and bulk formulated using the Kelvin equation. Figure 8 can be used to calculate the activation energy for the crystallization of the crystalliz-able component (here iPP) in a polymer blend. A semi-logarithmic plot of (t0.5) −1 versus (∆T)−1 as shown in Fig. 8, is linear in the Tc range investigated for neat iPP and iPP–NR blends. Figure 9 exhibits quantities (Ea) extracted from the slope ofthe semi-logarithmic plot of (t0.5) −1 versus (∆T)−1 for iPP in blends.', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', 'Figure 9 confirms that the Ea of iPP increases up to NR loading of 20%·w/w, and thereafter it shows a decrease, while for a blend with 50%·w/w of NR, it shows a substantial increase. The reason for this is the change in morphology (from droplet-matrix type to co-continuous type), as discussed in the following section.', 'SEM analysis shows droplet-matrix type morphology with gradual change to co-continuous type as a function of NR loading, (50%·w/w NR loading onwards) as evidenced by the SEM images shown in Fig. 10.', 'Figure 11 displays the optical micrographs of spherulite growth at 130 °C at thecooling rate of 10 °C/min in neat iPP, and iPP–NR blend with magnification of 10X.', 'Figure 11a confirms the presence of a well-defined “Maltese cross [14]” coin-ciding with the principal axis of crystal and extinction direction of the polarizer/analyzer, indicating uniform crystal growth. Higher concentrations of NR exhibit co-continuous morphology, preventing the actual growth of iPP spherulites and disappearing Maltase cross for the spherulite developed in the iPP–NR 50–50 composition [37, 38]. For a better understanding, magnified images 10× are shown in Fig. 12.', 'Spherulite growth rate (G) can be evaluated from the slope of the radius versus time curve, and Fig. 13a shows the growth rate for iPP, calculated from POM images. Figure 13b displays the contribution of the blend ratio on G over a different temperature range (124–136 °C). The spherulite growth rate reveals a decreasing pattern with increasing content of NR, and it is prominent at lower crystallization temperatures.', '@', '@', '@', '@', 'However, G is unaffected by the blend ratio at higher Tc. This can be due to the slower growth rate because of the high molecular mobility. TGA and DTA plots foriPP and iPP–NR blends are shown in Fig. 14a, b respectively.', 'The experiment was performed between temperature ranges of 30 to 700 °C. Fig-ure 14a shows that neat iPP has single-step degradation with high thermal stabil-ity (decomposition onset of 385 °C), while 50% of degradation occurs at 474 °C. With the addition of NR to the iPP matrix, a gradual decrement of onset of degra-dation reaching up to 308 °C can be observed (50%∙w/w of NR). It is noteworthy that blends of iPP–NR exhibit two degradation steps, indicating a highly immiscible system, which is obvious from the DTG curves (Fig. 3). Peak obtained in the range of 350 to 400 °C represents the degradation of NR, while iPP degradation occurred at 450 to 500 °C.', '@', '@', 'Conclusion', 'The crystallization kinetics, spherulitic growth rate and melting properties of iPP blended with non-polar counterpart have been evaluated and correlated to blend morphology. The addition of NR to iPP produces a gradual change in morphology from droplet-matrix to co-continuous type, and this resulted in a predominant con-tribution to the crystallization kinetics of iPP and the mutual interaction between the blend components. Decrease in Tm° with NR loading, point to the existence of weak attractive interactions between the blend components. This is also reflected in the spherulite growth rate. Even though the blending of iPP with other polymers has been studied and reported quite extensively, the effect of dynamic asymmetry on thermal properties and morphology development remains unexplored. Considering the commercial importance of iPP in various sectors, a detailed evaluation of the effect of molecular weight on thermal, mechanical and morphological properties is extremely important. The present study will usher in the development of iPP-based blends and nanocomposites with improved thermal and mechanical properties.', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', '@', 'Acknowledgements I would like to express my deepest gratitude to Professor Chin Han Chan, Universiti Teknologi MARA, Malaysia, whose guidance, encouragement, and unwavering support were instrumen-tal in the development of this research. Professor Chin Han Chan, not only provided invaluable academic insight but also inspired me with their passion for thermal analysis of polymers.
